Refuting Internal Compulsion in Kasb
Refutes al-Maragani's claim of internal compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb does not imply that the servant is inwardly compelled, and how jabr is defined.

Core Idea
Kasb means choice through God-created will, not internal compulsion.

The passage begins by quoting al-Maragani's view that the servant is inwardly compelled like a pen in a writer's hand. The commentator refutes this by defining jabr as acting against one's will and showing that in kasb, the servant's act follows his own created will, thus it is genuine choice.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Maragani's claim
Read the opening quote where al-Maragani states the servant is inwardly compelled and outwardly a chooser, like a pen in a writer's hand.
Notice the analogy: the pen has no real choice.
2
Define jabr from the refutation
The commentator defines jabr as 'occurrence of an act contrary to will'. Write down this definition and compare it with the Ash'ari position.
The definition is crucial for the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation
The commentator argues that God creates the act in accordance with the servant's created will, so the act is not contrary to the servant's will; thus it is not compulsion but genuine choice.
Focus on the point: God's creation of will does not cancel choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الكلام المتعلق بالكسب أعلاه استفدناه من العلامة المارغني ، ثم قال بعد ذلك : " وعليه فليس للحي : كالعبد في الأفعال الاختيارية إلا الاختيار في الظاهر والكسب، وإنما قلنا في الظاهر لأنه في الباطن مجبور لأن الله تعالى هو الذي خلق فيه الاختيار كما خلق الفعل والقدرة المقارنة له فهو مجبور في صورة مختار كالقلم في يد الكاتب، وقد رتب الله تعالى على الاختيار في الظاهر وعلى الكسب التكليف والثواب والعقاب والله التصرف في خلقه كيف شاء لا راد لحكمه ولا معقب لقضائه ولا يسأل عما يفعل فلله الحجة البالغة" أهـ. ولنا على هذه الفقرة تعليق ولذلك لم نثبته في الأصل فقوله إن العبد مجبور في الباطن غير مسلم، لأن الجبر معناه صدور الفعل على خلاف الإرادة، فإذا كنا نقول أصلاً بأن الفعل الاختياري لا يخلقه الله تعالى في العبد إلا موافقاً لإرادة العبد المخلوقة فيه نتيجة لعلم الله تعالى، كما وضحناه في تعليقاتنا أعلاه، فكيف يقال إن العبد مجبور باطنا مختار ظاهراً ؟! بل هذا الكلام غير مستقيم ، فكون الله تعالى خالقاً في العبد إرادته وفعله وقدرته لا يستلزم مطلقاً أن يكون العبد مجبوراً ، بل هذا هو الاختيار، لأنه تابع لإرادة العبد التي عليمها الله تعالى منذ الأزل ، وقد نبه إلى هذا العلامة المحقق الإمام السعد التفتازاني في شرح العقائد النسفية فقال : "فإن قيل : بعد تعميم علم الله تعالى وإرادته ، الجبر لازم قطعاً لأنهما إما أن يتعلقا بوجود الفعل فيجب أو بعدمه فيمتنع ، ولا اختيار مع الوجوب والامتناع، قلنا : يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركه باختياره، فلا إشكال. فإن قيل : فيكون فعله الاختياري واجباً أو ممتنعا وهذا ينافي الاختيار. قلنا : ممنوع، فإن الوجوب بالاختيار محقق للاختيار لا مناف ، وأيضاً منقوض بأفعال الباري جل ذكره لأن علمه وإرادته متعلقان بأفعاله فيلزم أن يكون فعله واجباً عليه أهـ كلامه وهو في غاية الإفادة. ومعنى عبارته الأخيرة : " أي ومعلوم أن فعله غير واجب عليه ، فيبطل كلامكم".
وهذه العبارة أعني "العبد مجبور في قالب مختار" قد فسرها الشيخ عليش في شرحه على السنوسية الكبرى فقال ما حاصله : العبد مجبور في الباطن ونفس الأمر على فعله الاختياري فإنه لا يمكنه تركه بعد خلق الشهوة والميل والإرادة والعزم عليه والقدرة عليه، وأما كونه مختاراً للفعل والترك في الظاهر فلأنه بحسب الظاهر يفعل إن شاء ويترك إن شاء، وفي نفس الأمر والحقيقة لا فعل له، وإنما الفعل الله سبحانه وتعالى وحده لا شريك له. هكذا فسر الشيخ عليش هذه العبارة هو معنى حسن ولطيف. أي إننا يمكن أن نتصور أن يريد العبد بعد أن يترك فعله وذلك بعد العزم عليه وخلق الشهوة والميل..... الخ ولكننا ندرك أن فعله في هذه الحالة واجب لأن الفعل عند القدرة والداعية والإرادة واجب، وهذا يكون مخالفا لإرادته التي تصورنا وقوعها منه بعد ذلك. فافهم.(س).
This speech regarding kasb above we benefited from the scholar al-Maragani. Then he said after that: 'Accordingly, the living being—like the slave—has only outward choice and kasb in the optional acts. We said 'outward' because inwardly he is compelled (majboor), for God Almighty created choice in him just as He created the act and the power concurrent with it. So he is compelled in the form of a chooser, like the pen in the hand of the writer. And God Almighty has ordained obligation, reward, and punishment upon the outward choice and upon kasb. And God acts in His creation as He wills; none can reverse His judgment, none can question His decree, and He is not asked about what He does. To God belongs the conclusive argument.' End quote. We have a comment on this paragraph and therefore we did not record it in the original. His statement that the slave is inwardly compelled is not acceptable, because jabr (compulsion) means the occurrence of an act contrary to will. Since we originally say that the optional act is not created by God in the slave except in accordance with the slave's will created in him as a result of God's knowledge, as we clarified in our comments above, how can it be said that the slave is inwardly compelled and outwardly a chooser? Rather, this statement is unsound. For the fact that God creates in the slave his will, his act, and his power does not absolutely entail that the slave is compelled; rather, this is choice, because it is consequent upon the slave's will which God knew from eternity. The verifying scholar Imam Sa'd al-Taftazani alerted to this in his commentary on al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya. He said: 'If it is said: After the generality of God's knowledge and will, compulsion is necessarily entailed, because they either relate to the existence of the act, so it is necessary, or to its non-existence, so it is impossible, and there is no choice with necessity and impossibility. We say: He knows and wills that the slave does or leaves it by his choice, so no problem. If it is said: Then his optional act is necessary or impossible, and this opposes choice. We say: Denied. For necessity by choice (al-wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar) actualizes choice, not opposes it. Also, it is refuted by the actions of the Exalted Creator, because His knowledge and will are related to His actions, so it would entail that His act is necessary upon Him.' End of his words, which are extremely beneficial. And the meaning of his last statement: 'And it is known that His act is not necessary upon Him, so your argument is invalid.' And this statement—i.e., 'the slave is compelled in the mold of a chooser'—Sheikh 'Alish interpreted in his commentary on al-Sanusiyya al-Kubra. He said, in summary: The slave is compelled inwardly and in truth regarding his optional act, for he cannot leave it after the creation of desire, inclination, will, determination, and power upon it. As for his being a chooser of act and omission outwardly, it is because outwardly he does if he wills and leaves if he wills, but in truth and reality he has no act; the act belongs to God alone, no partner. This is how Sheikh 'Alish interpreted this statement; it is a good and subtle meaning. That is, we can imagine that the slave wills after leaving his act, after determination and creation of desire and inclination, etc., but we realize that his act in this case is necessary, because the act when there is power, motive, and will is necessary, and this would be contrary to his will which we imagined to occur from him afterwards. So understand. (S)
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the view that the slave is inwardly compelled (majboor) while outwardly a chooser. It begins by quoting al-Maragani's statement from which the idea was taken. Al-Maragani claimed that the slave has only outward choice and kasb, because inwardly he is compelled like a pen in a writer's hand, since God creates choice and power in him. The author of this commentary (Said Foudah) then refutes this: compulsion means acting against one's will. But Ash'aris hold that God creates the act in accordance with the servant's created will, which God foreknew. Thus the servant is not compelled; his act follows his own will. To support this, the passage quotes Imam al-Taftazani from his commentary on al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya. Taftazani addresses the objection that God's universal knowledge and will necessitate compulsion. He replies that God knows and wills that the servant acts by his choice. When the objector persists that this makes the act necessary and thus not chosen, Taftazani responds that 'necessity by choice' (wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar) does not negate choice; indeed, it confirms it. He also refutes by analogy: if God's knowledge and will necessitated His own actions, that would imply God is compelled, which is false. The passage then presents Sheikh 'Alish's interpretation of the phrase 'the slave is compelled in the mold of a chooser.' 'Alish explains that the slave is inwardly compelled in the sense that once desire, will, and power are created, he cannot but act; but outwardly he appears to choose. The author finds this a good explanation: if we imagine that the slave wills to leave the act after determination, his act would still be necessary because of the presence of power and motive, so his imagined subsequent will would be contrary. Thus, the proper understanding of kasb avoids jabr.

Key Terms
جبر
اختيار
كسب
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'ari kasb means the servant is inwardly compelled (majboor) and only outwardly appears to choose.
Correction: The passage clarifies that jabr means acting against one's will. In kasb, God creates the act in accordance with the servant's will, so it is not compulsion but genuine choice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'jabr' (compulsion) mean?
A) Acting by force
B) Occurrence of an act contrary to will
C) Absence of power
D) Predestination

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari kasb does not entail internal compulsion because the act follows the servant's created will, not opposes it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your choices are an illusion, that God has already decided everything and you are just going through the motions? How does this lesson's explanation of kasb challenge or confirm that feeling? What would change in your daily decisions if you truly believed that your will matters and that your actions follow it, even though God creates both?
?
Think of a past sin that you regret. Did you ever blame God or fate, thinking you were 'compelled' to do it? After reading this lesson, can you accept that your will was genuinely involved? How can you take full responsibility for that sin now, knowing it followed your will? What concrete step can you take to repent and change your will going forward?
?
When you make a good deed, do you thank God for creating the act, or do you take full credit? This lesson says God creates the act in accordance with your will. How can you balance gratitude to God with a sense of personal effort and choice? Commit to a specific act of worship today where you consciously acknowledge both: 'I choose this, and God enables it.'

Taftazani's Defense of Choice and 'Alish's Interpretation
Presents Taftazani's argument and 'Alish's interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's argument that necessity by choice (wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar) does not negate freedom, and how Sheikh 'Alish interprets the phrase 'the slave is compelled in the mold of a chooser'.

Core Idea
Necessity by choice actualizes freedom; God's foreknowledge does not compel the servant.

Having refuted the idea of internal compulsion, the commentator now presents Taftazani's sophisticated response to the objection that God's universal knowledge and will necessitate compulsion. Taftazani argues that necessity arising from choice is compatible with freedom, using the example of God's own actions. Then, Sheikh 'Alish offers a subtle interpretation of the phrase 'compelled in the mold of a chooser,' explaining the necessity of the act once motives are complete.

Guided Path
1
Read Taftazani's objection and answer
Read Taftazani's response to the objection that God's knowledge and will necessitate compulsion. Note the two rounds of objection and answer.
Focus on the concept of 'necessity by choice' (wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar).
2
Examine the refutation by analogy
Taftazani refutes by pointing to God's own actions: if God's knowledge and will made His acts necessary, then God would be compelled, which is false.
This is a powerful rebuttal by analogy.
3
Understand 'Alish's interpretation
Sheikh 'Alish explains that the slave is compelled inwardly in the sense that once desire, will, and power are created, the act becomes necessary—but outwardly he appears to choose. The commentator finds this a good clarification.
Consider how this harmonizes with the rejection of jabr.

هذا الكلام المتعلق بالكسب أعلاه استفدناه من العلامة المارغني ، ثم قال بعد ذلك : " وعليه فليس للحي : كالعبد في الأفعال الاختيارية إلا الاختيار في الظاهر والكسب، وإنما قلنا في الظاهر لأنه في الباطن مجبور لأن الله تعالى هو الذي خلق فيه الاختيار كما خلق الفعل والقدرة المقارنة له فهو مجبور في صورة مختار كالقلم في يد الكاتب، وقد رتب الله تعالى على الاختيار في الظاهر وعلى الكسب التكليف والثواب والعقاب والله التصرف في خلقه كيف شاء لا راد لحكمه ولا معقب لقضائه ولا يسأل عما يفعل فلله الحجة البالغة" أهـ. ولنا على هذه الفقرة تعليق ولذلك لم نثبته في الأصل فقوله إن العبد مجبور في الباطن غير مسلم، لأن الجبر معناه صدور الفعل على خلاف الإرادة، فإذا كنا نقول أصلاً بأن الفعل الاختياري لا يخلقه الله تعالى في العبد إلا موافقاً لإرادة العبد المخلوقة فيه نتيجة لعلم الله تعالى، كما وضحناه في تعليقاتنا أعلاه، فكيف يقال إن العبد مجبور باطنا مختار ظاهراً ؟! بل هذا الكلام غير مستقيم ، فكون الله تعالى خالقاً في العبد إرادته وفعله وقدرته لا يستلزم مطلقاً أن يكون العبد مجبوراً ، بل هذا هو الاختيار، لأنه تابع لإرادة العبد التي عليمها الله تعالى منذ الأزل ، وقد نبه إلى هذا العلامة المحقق الإمام السعد التفتازاني في شرح العقائد النسفية فقال : "فإن قيل : بعد تعميم علم الله تعالى وإرادته ، الجبر لازم قطعاً لأنهما إما أن يتعلقا بوجود الفعل فيجب أو بعدمه فيمتنع ، ولا اختيار مع الوجوب والامتناع، قلنا : يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركه باختياره، فلا إشكال. فإن قيل : فيكون فعله الاختياري واجباً أو ممتنعا وهذا ينافي الاختيار. قلنا : ممنوع، فإن الوجوب بالاختيار محقق للاختيار لا مناف ، وأيضاً منقوض بأفعال الباري جل ذكره لأن علمه وإرادته متعلقان بأفعاله فيلزم أن يكون فعله واجباً عليه أهـ كلامه وهو في غاية الإفادة. ومعنى عبارته الأخيرة : " أي ومعلوم أن فعله غير واجب عليه ، فيبطل كلامكم".
وهذه العبارة أعني "العبد مجبور في قالب مختار" قد فسرها الشيخ عليش في شرحه على السنوسية الكبرى فقال ما حاصله : العبد مجبور في الباطن ونفس الأمر على فعله الاختياري فإنه لا يمكنه تركه بعد خلق الشهوة والميل والإرادة والعزم عليه والقدرة عليه، وأما كونه مختاراً للفعل والترك في الظاهر فلأنه بحسب الظاهر يفعل إن شاء ويترك إن شاء، وفي نفس الأمر والحقيقة لا فعل له، وإنما الفعل الله سبحانه وتعالى وحده لا شريك له. هكذا فسر الشيخ عليش هذه العبارة هو معنى حسن ولطيف. أي إننا يمكن أن نتصور أن يريد العبد بعد أن يترك فعله وذلك بعد العزم عليه وخلق الشهوة والميل..... الخ ولكننا ندرك أن فعله في هذه الحالة واجب لأن الفعل عند القدرة والداعية والإرادة واجب، وهذا يكون مخالفا لإرادته التي تصورنا وقوعها منه بعد ذلك. فافهم.(س).
This speech regarding kasb above we benefited from the scholar al-Maragani. Then he said after that: 'Accordingly, the living being—like the slave—has only outward choice and kasb in the optional acts. We said 'outward' because inwardly he is compelled (majboor), for God Almighty created choice in him just as He created the act and the power concurrent with it. So he is compelled in the form of a chooser, like the pen in the hand of the writer. And God Almighty has ordained obligation, reward, and punishment upon the outward choice and upon kasb. And God acts in His creation as He wills; none can reverse His judgment, none can question His decree, and He is not asked about what He does. To God belongs the conclusive argument.' End quote. We have a comment on this paragraph and therefore we did not record it in the original. His statement that the slave is inwardly compelled is not acceptable, because jabr (compulsion) means the occurrence of an act contrary to will. Since we originally say that the optional act is not created by God in the slave except in accordance with the slave's will created in him as a result of God's knowledge, as we clarified in our comments above, how can it be said that the slave is inwardly compelled and outwardly a chooser? Rather, this statement is unsound. For the fact that God creates in the slave his will, his act, and his power does not absolutely entail that the slave is compelled; rather, this is choice, because it is consequent upon the slave's will which God knew from eternity. The verifying scholar Imam Sa'd al-Taftazani alerted to this in his commentary on al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya. He said: 'If it is said: After the generality of God's knowledge and will, compulsion is necessarily entailed, because they either relate to the existence of the act, so it is necessary, or to its non-existence, so it is impossible, and there is no choice with necessity and impossibility. We say: He knows and wills that the slave does or leaves it by his choice, so no problem. If it is said: Then his optional act is necessary or impossible, and this opposes choice. We say: Denied. For necessity by choice (al-wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar) actualizes choice, not opposes it. Also, it is refuted by the actions of the Exalted Creator, because His knowledge and will are related to His actions, so it would entail that His act is necessary upon Him.' End of his words, which are extremely beneficial. And the meaning of his last statement: 'And it is known that His act is not necessary upon Him, so your argument is invalid.' And this statement—i.e., 'the slave is compelled in the mold of a chooser'—Sheikh 'Alish interpreted in his commentary on al-Sanusiyya al-Kubra. He said, in summary: The slave is compelled inwardly and in truth regarding his optional act, for he cannot leave it after the creation of desire, inclination, will, determination, and power upon it. As for his being a chooser of act and omission outwardly, it is because outwardly he does if he wills and leaves if he wills, but in truth and reality he has no act; the act belongs to God alone, no partner. This is how Sheikh 'Alish interpreted this statement; it is a good and subtle meaning. That is, we can imagine that the slave wills after leaving his act, after determination and creation of desire and inclination, etc., but we realize that his act in this case is necessary, because the act when there is power, motive, and will is necessary, and this would be contrary to his will which we imagined to occur from him afterwards. So understand. (S)
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the view that the slave is inwardly compelled (majboor) while outwardly a chooser. It begins by quoting al-Maragani's statement from which the idea was taken. Al-Maragani claimed that the slave has only outward choice and kasb, because inwardly he is compelled like a pen in a writer's hand, since God creates choice and power in him. The author of this commentary (Said Foudah) then refutes this: compulsion means acting against one's will. But Ash'aris hold that God creates the act in accordance with the servant's created will, which God foreknew. Thus the servant is not compelled; his act follows his own will. To support this, the passage quotes Imam al-Taftazani from his commentary on al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya. Taftazani addresses the objection that God's universal knowledge and will necessitate compulsion. He replies that God knows and wills that the servant acts by his choice. When the objector persists that this makes the act necessary and thus not chosen, Taftazani responds that 'necessity by choice' (wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar) does not negate choice; indeed, it confirms it. He also refutes by analogy: if God's knowledge and will necessitated His own actions, that would imply God is compelled, which is false. The passage then presents Sheikh 'Alish's interpretation of the phrase 'the slave is compelled in the mold of a chooser.' 'Alish explains that the slave is inwardly compelled in the sense that once desire, will, and power are created, he cannot but act; but outwardly he appears to choose. The author finds this a good explanation: if we imagine that the slave wills to leave the act after determination, his act would still be necessary because of the presence of power and motive, so his imagined subsequent will would be contrary. Thus, the proper understanding of kasb avoids jabr.

Key Terms
وجوب بالاختيار
جبر
اختيار
علم الله
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an act is necessary due to God's foreknowledge and will, it cannot be genuinely chosen.
Correction: Taftazani argues that necessity by choice (wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar) is compatible with freedom and actually confirms it, as shown by the example of God's own actions.

Checkpoint
What does Taftazani mean by 'necessity by choice' (wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar)?
A) The act is necessary because God forces it
B) The act is necessary because the agent chooses it, which confirms choice
C) The act is impossible
D) The act is optional

Key Takeaway
Necessity by choice (wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar) is compatible with genuine freedom, and God's foreknowledge does not compel the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'It was decreed, so I had no choice'? How does Taftazani's point that God knows and wills that you act by your choice dismantle that excuse? Will you repent now and take full responsibility for one specific sin you've been blaming on fate?
?
The lesson says necessity by choice (wujub bi'l-ikhtiyar) actualizes freedom, not negates it. Think of a habit or action you felt 'forced' to do because of your desires. In reality, your own will and power were complete. Will you admit that you chose it, and ask Allah for strength to choose differently? What is one small change you will make today?
?
'Alish says the slave is compelled inwardly but outwardly appears to choose. Does this description comfort you or disturb you? If you truly believe that your act is necessary given your motives, will you stop blaming others—your upbringing, environment, or circumstances—for your wrong choices? Name one person you have blamed unjustly, and decide to forgive them and own your own fault.

Refuting Inner Compulsion and Taftazani's Response
Refutation of inner compulsion with Taftazani's proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the slave is inwardly compelled is false, and how Taftazani resolves the apparent conflict between divine will and human choice.

Core Idea
Divine will and human choice coexist; compulsion is not entailed.

The author tackles a common objection: if God creates everything, including our will, aren't we just compelled? He defines real compulsion, then shows that God's creating our will actually enables genuine choice. Taftazani's elegant argument clarifies that divine knowledge and will do not force actions but rather ensure they occur by our own choice.

Guided Path
1
Define true compulsion (jabr)
Read the first line where the author defines jabr as action contrary to will. This sets the standard.
Remember: not every determination is compulsion.
2
Follow the refutation of inner compulsion
The author argues that Allah creates the slave's will and action in harmony, not force. The slave acts according to his own will, which is knowledge by Allah from eternity.
Focus on the phrase 'tabi' li-iradat al-'abd'.
3
Study Taftazani's objection and answer
Taftazani presents two objections: (1) God's knowledge/will entails necessity or impossibility, negating choice. (2) The action becomes necessary, contradicting choice. He gives two answers: (1) God knows and wills the slave to act by choice. (2) Necessity by choice actualizes choice; analogy with God's own actions.
Notice the parallel: God's actions are not necessary upon Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله على هذه الفقرة تعليق ولذلك لم نثبته في الأصل فقوله إن العبد مجبور في الباطن غير مسلم، لأن الجبر معناه صدور الفعل على خلاف الإرادة، فإذا كنا نقول أصلاً بأن الفعل الاختياري لا يخلقه الله تعالى في العبد إلا موافقاً لإرادة العبد المخلوقة فيه نتيجة لعلم الله تعالى، كما وضحناه في تعليقاتنا أعلاه، فكيف يقال إن العبد مجبور باطنا مختار ظاهراً ؟! بل هذا الكلام غير مستقيم ، فكون الله تعالى خالقاً في العبد إرادته وفعله وقدرته لا يستلزم مطلقاً أن يكون العبد مجبوراً ، بل هذا هو الاختيار، لأنه تابع لإرادة العبد التي عليمها الله تعالى منذ الأزل ، وقد نبه إلى هذا العلامة المحقق الإمام السعد التفتازاني في شرح العقائد النسفية فقال : "فإن قيل : بعد تعميم علم الله تعالى وإرادته ، الجبر لازم قطعاً لأنهما إما أن يتعلقا بوجود الفعل فيجب أو بعدمه فيمتنع ، ولا اختيار مع الوجوب والامتناع، قلنا : يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركه باختياره، فلا إشكال. فإن قيل : فيكون فعله الاختياري واجباً أو ممتنعا وهذا ينافي الاختيار. قلنا : ممنوع، فإن الوجوب بالاختيار محقق للاختيار لا مناف، وأيضاً منقوض بأفعال الباري جل ذكره لأن علمه وإرادته متعلقان بأفعاله فيلزم أن يكون فعله واجباً عليه أهـ كلامه وهو في غاية الإفادة. ومعنى عبارته الأخيرة : " أي ومعلوم أن فعله غير واجب عليه ، فيبطل كلامكم".
And we have a comment on this paragraph, and therefore we did not include it in the original text. His statement that the slave is compelled inwardly is not accepted, because jabr means the occurrence of an action contrary to will. Since we already hold that the voluntary action is created by Allah in the slave only in accordance with the slave's will created in him as a result of Allah's knowledge, as we clarified in our comments above, how can it be said that the slave is compelled inwardly and free outwardly? Rather, this statement is not upright. For Allah being the creator in the slave of his will, his action, and his power does not entail at all that the slave is compelled. Rather, this is choice (ikhtiyar), because it follows the slave's will which Allah has known from eternity. The verifying scholar Imam Sa'd al-Taftazani drew attention to this in his commentary on al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya, saying: 'If it is said: After the encompassing of Allah's knowledge and will, jabr is necessarily entailed, because they either attach to the existence of the action, so it becomes necessary, or to its non-existence, so it becomes impossible, and there is no choice with necessity and impossibility. We say: He knows and wills that the slave does it or leaves it by his own choice, so there is no problem. If it is said: Then his voluntary action becomes necessary or impossible, and this contradicts choice. We say: It is denied, for necessity by choice actualizes choice, not contradiction. Moreover, it is undermined by the actions of the Exalted Lord, because His knowledge and will are attached to His actions, so it would entail that His action is necessary upon Him.' End of his words, which are extremely beneficial. And the meaning of his last statement: 'And it is known that His action is not necessary upon Him, so your statement is invalid.'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the slave is compelled inwardly while free outwardly. The author first defines jabr as the occurrence of an action contrary to will. He then argues that Allah creates the slave's will, action, and power in accordance with His eternal knowledge, so the slave's action follows his own will, thus it is not jabr but choice. He supports this by citing Taftazani's objection-answer: the objection that God's encompassing knowledge and will entail jabr is answered by stating that God knows and wills the slave to act by his own choice. The second objection that the action becomes necessary or impossible is answered by noting that necessity arising from choice does not negate choice, and it is analogous to God's own actions, which are not necessary upon Him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That because God creates our will, we are compelled and have no real choice.
Correction: Compulsion (jabr) means acting against one's will. Since God creates our will in accord with His knowledge, our actions follow our own will, which is the essence of choice.

Checkpoint

A) Action without power
B) Action contrary to will
C) Action determined by God
D) Action with hesitation

Key Takeaway
Divine creation of human will does not negate human choice; rather, it establishes it, because choice is defined by action in accordance with one's own will.

How does Taftazani's argument that 'necessity by choice actualizes choice' challenge that excuse? This week, when you face a temptation, consciously remind yourself that your will is real and your action is your own. Will you do that?
?

The passage says that being created in you does not negate your choice—so are you willing to own every sin and every good deed as truly yours, with no escape into 'it was destined'?
?
The author uses the example of Allah's own actions to show that knowledge and will do not compel. When you reflect on your own life, which actions do you tend to feel are 'out of your hands'—perhaps your anger, your laziness, or your addictions? Will you stop treating those as compelled and start treating them as choices you can repent from and change?

Shaykh 'Illaysh on Compulsion and Freedom in Kasb
Shaykh 'Illaysh explains the dual aspect of the slave's action in kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Shaykh 'Illaysh's nuanced interpretation of how the slave is inwardly compelled yet outwardly free, resolving the apparent tension in the Ash'ari concept of kasb.

Core Idea
The slave is inwardly compelled, outwardly free — both are true.

This passage presents a subtle gloss by Shaykh 'Illaysh on a famous expression describing the human act. The phrase 'compelled in a mold of free choice' seems paradoxical. 'Illaysh clarifies that inwardly, after the creation of desire and power, the act is necessary; outwardly, the slave appears to choose. Let us read his explanation and the author's further reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key terms: majbur (compelled) and mukhtar (free). Pay attention to the distinction between 'inwardly' and 'outwardly'.
The passage uses 'batin' (inward) and 'zahir' (outward).
2
Identify the two aspects
Locate the description of inward compulsion (majbur fi al-batin) and outward freedom (mukhtar fi al-zahir). Understand what each entails.
Inwardly: cannot leave the action after desire, inclination, will, resolve, and power are created. Outwardly: does if he wills, leaves if he wills.
3
Grasp the subtle nuance
The author adds a reflection: we can imagine the slave willing to leave the action after resolve, but we perceive the action is then necessary, contradicting that will. This shows the deep harmony of the doctrine.
This nuance prevents misunderstanding that the slave has no will at all.
4
Connect to wider kalam
Consider how this interpretation upholds moral accountability while affirming Allah's exclusive creative power. The outward freedom is real for reward and punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه العبارة أعني "العبد مجبور في قالب مختار" قد فسرها الشيخ عليش في شرحه على السنوسية الكبرى فقال ما حاصله : العبد مجبور في الباطن ونفس الأمر على فعله الاختياري فإنه لا يمكنه تركه بعد خلق الشهوة والميل والإرادة والعزم عليه والقدرة عليه، وأما كونه مختاراً للفعل والترك في الظاهر فلأنه بحسب الظاهر يفعل إن شاء ويترك إن شاء، وفي نفس الأمر والحقيقة لا فعل له، وإنما الفعل الله سبحانه وتعالى وحده لا شريك له. هكذا فسر الشيخ عليش هذه العبارة هو معنى حسن ولطيف. أي إننا يمكن أن نتصور أن يريد العبد بعد أن يترك فعله وذلك بعد العزم عليه وخلق الشهوة والميل..... الخ ولكننا ندرك أن فعله في هذه الحالة واجب لأن الفعل عند القدرة والداعية والإرادة واجب، وهذا يكون مخالفا لإرادته التي تصورنا وقوعها منه بعد ذلك. فافهم.(س).
And this statement, namely 'the slave is compelled in a mold of free choice', was interpreted by Shaykh 'Illaysh in his commentary on al-Sanusiyya al-Kubra, and said in summary: The slave is compelled inwardly and in actual reality with regard to his voluntary action, for he cannot leave it after the creation of desire, inclination, will, resolve, and power upon it. As for his being free in action and omission outwardly, it is because outwardly he does if he wills and leaves if he wills, but in actual reality and truth he has no action; the action belongs only to Allah, Exalted and Glorified, alone with no partner. Thus Shaykh 'Illaysh interpreted this statement; it is a good and subtle meaning. That is, we can imagine that the slave wills to leave his action after the resolve upon it and the creation of desire, inclination... etc., but we perceive that his action in this case is necessary, because the action with power and motive and will is necessary, and this would contradict his imagined will. So understand.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage provides Shaykh 'Illaysh's interpretation of the phrase 'the slave is compelled in a mold of free choice'. He explains that inwardly the slave is compelled because once desire, inclination, will, resolve, and power are created, he cannot leave the action. Outwardly he appears to have choice because he seems to do or leave as he wills. However, in reality, the action is from Allah alone. The author then adds a subtle point: we can imagine the slave willing to leave the action after the resolve, but we know that when power, motive, and will are present, the action becomes necessary, so it would contradict his supposed will. This shows the nuance of the Ash'ari position on kasb.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The slave has no real choice; everything is predetermined and human actions are illusory.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while inwardly compelled, the slave is outwardly free in action and omission, preserving real choice for accountability.

Checkpoint

C) Outwardly, because his actions appear compelled

Key Takeaway

The passage says that after desire and will are created, the action becomes necessary. Recall a sin you committed with full will. Could you have really avoided it at that moment? If not, how does this understanding transform your repentance and reliance on Allah?

The Philosophers' Doctrine of Causation and the Sunni Alternative
Sanusi defines causation and presents the Sunni alternative of concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' concept of causation and why Sunni scholars affirm that created events are merely concomitant, not causally connected.

Core Idea
True causation implies necessity; but only Allah creates, and events merely accompany each other.

Sanusi begins by rejecting the idea that Allah creates by causation. He then explains what the philosophers mean by a cause: something that necessitates an effect without will or condition. Using their own example of a finger and a ring, he shows the Sunni alternative—both movements are directly created by Allah and are simply concomitant in existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on causation
Read the Arabic passage from 'ويستحيل' to 'الملازم لا المؤثر' and its translation. Focus on the definition of al-ta'lil and the cause.
2
Identify the philosophers' definition
Note what the philosophers say a cause is: something from which an act proceeds necessarily, without dependence on conditions or obstacles.
This definition is central to their worldview.
3
Understand the Sunni response
See how Sanusi contrasts this with the Sunni position: the finger and ring movements are both created by Allah, and they are only concomitant, not causally linked.
The term 'cause' may be used leniently to mean 'customary concomitant'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل على الله تعالى إيجاد شيء من العالم بالتعليل بأن يكون ذاته تعالى علة في إيجاد المخلوقات من غير إرادة وقدرة فالقائلون بذلك أهل العلة من الفلاسفة وحقيقة العلة عندهم ما يأتي منها الفعل دون الترك من غير توقف على وجود شرط ولا انتفاء مانع ، قالوا مثال ذلك في الشاهد حركة الإصبع فإنها علة حركة الخاتم ، ويلزم من حركة الإصبع حركة الخاتم، ومن عدمها عدم حركة الخاتم ، وهذا عندهم، وأما عند أهل الحق فحركة الخاتم مع الإصبع مخلوقان الله تعالى. وهما متلازمان وجوداً بإيجاد الله تعالى، وليس حركة الإصبع خالقاً لحركة الخاتم، ولا العكس، بل هما متلازمان وجوداً، وقد يتسامح البعض فيطلق على أحدهما اسم السبب، ويريد به السبب العادي، بمعنى الملازم لا المؤثر. وردّ أهل السنة على أهل العلة فقالوا لهم إن قلتم الذات قديمة يلزم قدم العالم لأن المعلول لا يتأخر عن علته وإن قلتم حادث فيفتقر إلى فاعل والفاعل لا يكون عندكم إلا علة .وهكذا فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل وهما محالان.
And it is impossible for Allah Almighty to bring into existence anything of the world by causation (al-ta'lil), meaning that His Essence is a cause (illa) in bringing into existence the created things without will and power. Those who say this are the people of causation among the philosophers. The reality of the cause according to them is that from which the act proceeds without its omission, without being dependent on the existence of a condition nor the absence of an obstacle. They said an example of that in the witnessed realm is the movement of the finger, for it is the cause of the movement of the ring, and the movement of the finger necessitates the movement of the ring, and its absence necessitates the absence of the movement of the ring. This is according to them. As for according to the people of truth, the movement of the ring with the finger are both created by Allah Almighty, and they are two concomitants in existence by the creation of Allah Almighty. The movement of the finger is not a creator of the movement of the ring, nor the opposite, rather they are two concomitants in existence. Some may be lenient and apply to one of them the name 'cause' (sabab), meaning by it the customary cause, i.e., the concomitant not the efficient. And the Ahl al-Sunnah responded to the people of causation, saying to them: If you say the essence is eternal, then the eternity of the world follows because the effect does not lag behind its cause; and if you say it is temporal, then it needs an agent, and the agent according to you is nothing but a cause. Thus circularity or infinite regress follows, and both are impossible.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of causation (al-ta'lil) as held by some philosophers, who claim that God's essence is a cause for the existence of the world without will and power. Sanusi first defines their concept of a cause: it is something from which an act proceeds necessarily, without the possibility of omission, and without dependence on conditions or obstacles. They give the example of the finger and the ring: the finger's movement is the cause of the ring's movement, and when the finger moves, the ring necessarily moves. The Ahl al-Sunnah reject this, stating that both movements are created by Allah and are merely concomitant in existence. The term 'cause' may be used in a customary sense to mean a concomitant, not an efficient cause. Then Sanusi presents the refutation: if the cause (God's essence) is eternal, then the effect (the world) must be eternal as well, since an effect cannot be separated from its cause. But if the cause is temporal, then it requires another agent, which in their view is also a cause, leading to an infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. Thus the doctrine of causation is invalidated.

Key Terms
التعليل
العلة
السبب العادي
الملازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Sunni Islam denies any regular patterns in nature.
Correction: Sunni scholars affirm that events regularly accompany each other by Allah's custom (sunnatullah), but deny that the earlier event necessarily causes the later one.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers mentioned by Sanusi, what is a cause (al-illa)?
A) Something that usually precedes an effect
B) Something from which an act proceeds necessarily, without dependence on conditions or obstacles
C) Something that is free to act or not act
D) The primary substance of the universe

Key Takeaway
True causation implies necessity and is denied; events are concomitant by Allah's direct creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a clear cause and effect in your life—like hard work leading to success—do you truly believe that Allah creates both your effort and the outcome, or does your heart still rely on the 'cause' as if it produces the effect on its own? How can you train your heart to see Allah's direct hand in every moment?
?
Think of a recent event where you felt entitled to a result because of your actions. Does the lesson's teaching that Allah is not compelled by any cause shake your sense of entitlement? Will you now thank Him for every blessing as a pure gift, not a deserved outcome?
?
The philosophers imagined a god who creates by necessity, without will. Do you ever live as if Allah is obligated to answer your prayers because of your deeds? What would it mean to repent from this subtle belief and surrender to His absolute freedom in giving and withholding?

The Refutation of Causation: Circularity and Infinite Regress
Sanusi presents a logical refutation of causation using a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the philosophers' doctrine of causation, showing that it leads either to the eternity of the world or to an infinite regress or circularity.

Core Idea
If causation is real, the effect must be eternal or lead to an impossible chain.

Sanusi now directly refutes the philosophers' position. He presents a dilemma: if God's essence is considered a cause, then either that cause is eternal or temporal. Both options lead to contradictions—if eternal, the world must be eternal; if temporal, it requires another cause, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. Both are impossible, so the doctrine of causation is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'وردّ أهل السنة' to the end and its translation. Focus on the logical structure.
2
Identify the two hypotheses
Note the two conditional statements: if the cause is eternal, then the effect is eternal; if the cause is temporal, then it needs another agent, which is also a cause.
This is a classic dilemma.
3
Understand the impossible consequences
See how the first leads to the eternity of the world (which is false), and the second leads to circularity or infinite regress (both impossible).
The argument shows the philosophers' view collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل على الله تعالى إيجاد شيء من العالم بالتعليل بأن يكون ذاته تعالى علة في إيجاد المخلوقات من غير إرادة وقدرة فالقائلون بذلك أهل العلة من الفلاسفة وحقيقة العلة عندهم ما يأتي منها الفعل دون الترك من غير توقف على وجود شرط ولا انتفاء مانع ، قالوا مثال ذلك في الشاهد حركة الإصبع فإنها علة حركة الخاتم ، ويلزم من حركة الإصبع حركة الخاتم، ومن عدمها عدم حركة الخاتم ، وهذا عندهم، وأما عند أهل الحق فحركة الخاتم مع الإصبع مخلوقان الله تعالى. وهما متلازمان وجوداً بإيجاد الله تعالى، وليس حركة الإصبع خالقاً لحركة الخاتم، ولا العكس، بل هما متلازمان وجوداً، وقد يتسامح البعض فيطلق على أحدهما اسم السبب، ويريد به السبب العادي، بمعنى الملازم لا المؤثر. وردّ أهل السنة على أهل العلة فقالوا لهم إن قلتم الذات قديمة يلزم قدم العالم لأن المعلول لا يتأخر عن علته وإن قلتم حادث فيفتقر إلى فاعل والفاعل لا يكون عندكم إلا علة .وهكذا فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل وهما محالان.
And it is impossible for Allah Almighty to bring into existence anything of the world by causation (al-ta'lil), meaning that His Essence is a cause (illa) in bringing into existence the created things without will and power. Those who say this are the people of causation among the philosophers. The reality of the cause according to them is that from which the act proceeds without its omission, without being dependent on the existence of a condition nor the absence of an obstacle. They said an example of that in the witnessed realm is the movement of the finger, for it is the cause of the movement of the ring, and the movement of the finger necessitates the movement of the ring, and its absence necessitates the absence of the movement of the ring. This is according to them. As for according to the people of truth, the movement of the ring with the finger are both created by Allah Almighty, and they are two concomitants in existence by the creation of Allah Almighty. The movement of the finger is not a creator of the movement of the ring, nor the opposite, rather they are two concomitants in existence. Some may be lenient and apply to one of them the name 'cause' (sabab), meaning by it the customary cause, i.e., the concomitant not the efficient. And the Ahl al-Sunnah responded to the people of causation, saying to them: If you say the essence is eternal, then the eternity of the world follows because the effect does not lag behind its cause; and if you say it is temporal, then it needs an agent, and the agent according to you is nothing but a cause. Thus circularity or infinite regress follows, and both are impossible.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of causation (al-ta'lil) as held by some philosophers, who claim that God's essence is a cause for the existence of the world without will and power. Sanusi first defines their concept of a cause: it is something from which an act proceeds necessarily, without the possibility of omission, and without dependence on conditions or obstacles. They give the example of the finger and the ring: the finger's movement is the cause of the ring's movement, and when the finger moves, the ring necessarily moves. The Ahl al-Sunnah reject this, stating that both movements are created by Allah and are merely concomitant in existence. The term 'cause' may be used in a customary sense to mean a concomitant, not an efficient cause. Then Sanusi presents the refutation: if the cause (God's essence) is eternal, then the effect (the world) must be eternal as well, since an effect cannot be separated from its cause. But if the cause is temporal, then it requires another agent, which in their view is also a cause, leading to an infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. Thus the doctrine of causation is invalidated.

Key Terms
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the refutation depends on assuming the world is temporal without proof.
Correction: The refutation is logical: it shows that the philosophers' own premises lead to contradictions, regardless of the world's temporality.

Checkpoint
What follows if the cause is eternal according to the refutation?
A) The world will be created later
B) The world must be eternal
C) Nothing follows
D) The world becomes temporal

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of causation is refuted because it leads either to the eternity of the world or to circularity/infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your relationship with Allah operates on a cause-and-effect basis, such that certain actions automatically guarantee certain outcomes? How does this lesson's refutation of causation challenge you to rely solely on Allah's will and mercy rather than on a perceived causal system?
?
The philosophers' error was to apply a human framework of causation to Allah. In what areas of your life might you be imposing your own expectations of how Allah should act based on your limited understanding? What would it mean to submit to a God whose ways are not bound by causation as you understand it?

The Impossibility of Ignorance for Allah
Defines ignorance, its two types, and declares them impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ignorance in any form — compound or simple, along with all its related concepts — is absolutely impossible for Allah, and how this is grounded in the nature of knowledge.

Core Idea
Ignorance in every form is a deficiency; Allah is absolutely free from all deficiency.

Sanusi now turns from affirming knowledge to negating its opposite: ignorance. He defines ignorance precisely, distinguishes its two types — compound and simple — and then declares both, along with every mental state that implies ignorance, impossible for Allah. This lesson helps you see why even a trace of doubt or forgetfulness cannot be attributed to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once to let the flow sink in. Then read the English translation to understand the full meaning.
The passage is dense with technical terms; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the two types of ignorance
Find where compound and simple ignorance are defined. Note the crucial difference: compound ignorance involves being unaware of one’s own ignorance.
This distinction is key for understanding why both are impossible for Allah.
3
Note the extension to related concepts
The passage lists doubt, conjecture, illusion, heedlessness, inattention, forgetfulness, and also categories like ‘theoretical’ or ‘acquired’ knowledge as impossible. Ask yourself why each of these implies ignorance or deficiency.
Each concept entails a lack of complete and perfect knowledge.
4
Understand the logical notes
Read the two notes: one on the objects of knowledge (necessary, impossible, possible) and one on the type of opposition between knowledge and ignorance. This clarifies that knowledge is positive and excludes ignorance completely.
5
Synthesize the ruling
Combine all parts: ignorance in any form, and any concept in its meaning, is impossible for Allah because His knowledge is perfect and existential.
This affirms a key divine attribute: absolute omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل الجهل عليه تعالى: وهو ضد العلم والجهل قسمان مركب وبسيط: فحقيقة المركب تصور الشيء على خلاف ما هو به، وهو أن يجهل الشيء ويجهل جهله به، فهو مركب من جهلين. والبسيط أن يجهل الشيء ويعلم أنه جاهل به وحقيقته عدم العلم بالشيء فيما شأنه أن يعلم. هذان القسمان مستحيلان على الله تعالى. ويستحيل عليه أيضا ما في معنى الجهل، كالشك والظن والوهم والذهول والغفلة والنسيان وكون علمه ضرورياً أو نظرياً أو بديهياً أو معرفة، لأن المعرفة ما تقدمها جهل على قول بعض العلماء، أو كونه يقينياً لأن اليقين ما ثبت بالدليل، أو كونه نظرياً أو كسبياً. ملاحظة: المعلوم يشمل الواجب والمستحيل والجائز. ملاحظة: التقابل بين العلم والجهل المركب تقابل بين الضدين، وبين العلم والجهل البسيط من باب عدم العلم والملكة. وحيث ثبت العلم انتفت حقيقة الجهل والملكة فالعلم وجودي.
And ignorance is impossible for Him, exalted: and it is the opposite of knowledge. And ignorance is two types: compound and simple. The reality of compound ignorance is conceiving a thing contrary to what it is, and it is that one is ignorant of the thing and ignorant of his ignorance of it, so it is compounded of two ignorances. The simple is that one is ignorant of the thing and knows that he is ignorant of it, and its reality is the absence of knowledge of a thing in something that should be known. These two types are impossible for Allah, exalted. And also impossible for Him is what is in the meaning of ignorance, such as doubt, conjecture, illusion, heedlessness, inattention, forgetfulness, and that His knowledge be necessary or theoretical or self-evident or acquaintance, because acquaintance is what is preceded by ignorance according to some scholars, or that it be certain because certainty is what is established by evidence, or that it be theoretical or acquired. Note: The known includes the necessary, the impossible, and the possible. Note: The opposition between knowledge and compound ignorance is opposition of contraries, and between knowledge and simple ignorance is from the category of absence and capability. And when knowledge is established, the reality of ignorance and capability are negated, so knowledge is existential.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition of the impossibility of ignorance (الجهل) for Allah. It begins by defining ignorance as the opposite of knowledge, then classifies it into two types: مركب (compound) and بسيط (simple). Compound ignorance is explained as conceiving something contrary to its true nature, combined with unawareness of one's own ignorance—thus two ignorances: ignorance of the thing and ignorance of that ignorance. Simple ignorance is being unaware of something while knowing that one lacks that knowledge; its essence is the absence of knowledge in a subject that should know. Both types are declared absolutely impossible (مستحيلان) for Allah. The passage then extends the impossibility to all mental states that resemble ignorance, listing: doubt (شك), conjecture (ظن), illusion (وهم), heedlessness (ذهول), inattention (غفلة), forgetfulness (نسيان), and also that Allah's knowledge be classified as necessary (ضروري), theoretical (نظري), self-evident (بديهي), or acquaintance (معرفة) because these entail a prior state of ignorance, except that according to some scholars knowledge is not preceded by ignorance; similarly that it be certain (يقيني) because certainty implies derivation from a proof, or that it be acquired (كسبي) or theoretical (نظري). Then two notes are added: first, that the objects of knowledge include the necessary (واجب), impossible (مستحيل), and possible (جائز). Second, the logical opposition between knowledge and compound ignorance is that of contraries (ضدين), while between knowledge and simple ignorance it is from the category of absence and capability (عدم الملكة) because simple ignorance is the absence of knowledge where it should exist. Once knowledge is present, both ignorance and the very capability of ignorance cease, proving that knowledge is an existential attribute (وجودي).

Key Terms
الجهل
المركب
البسيط
الشك
الظن
الوهم
الذهول
الغفلة
النسيان
معرفة
يقيني
كسبي
وجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that ignorance is simply not knowing, and that there is no difference between being unaware and being aware of one's ignorance.
Correction: The passage distinguishes compound ignorance (being ignorant and unaware of it) from simple ignorance (being ignorant but aware). Both are impossible for Allah, but the logical opposition differs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between compound ignorance and simple ignorance?
A) Compound ignorance involves knowing one is ignorant, simple does not
B) Compound ignorance involves two layers: being ignorant and unaware of that ignorance; simple ignorance is being ignorant while aware of it
C) Simple ignorance is worse than compound
D) Both are the same in effect

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot possess any form of ignorance, whether compound, simple, or any related mental state, because His knowledge is perfect and existential, leaving no room for deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that compound ignorance is being wrong and not knowing you are wrong. Where in your understanding of Allah's attributes might you hold a confident but mistaken belief? Will you examine your sources and correct yourself before you meet Him?
?
The passage says forgetfulness is impossible for Allah. Yet how often do you forget Him, even in your prayers or while reading His book? If your Lord never forgets you, will you strive to be more mindful so your remembrance of Him is not just occasional but constant?
?
Allah's knowledge is perfect—no doubt, no conjecture, no uncertainty. When you have doubts about your faith, do you rest in them or actively seek certainty? Commit to a specific step this week to replace one doubt with certain knowledge from revelation.

The Possible in God's Right: Definition and Scope
Definition of al-ja'iz for God and its rational rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the possible (al-ja'iz) in relation to God and why creating all possibles at once is impossible.

Core Idea
God may create or leave any possible thing; He is not obligated to do any.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of 'the permissible' in God’s actions. Before discussing specific examples, we must grasp what it means for something to be possible for God and the logical limits involved. The passage clarifies that while God can do anything possible, He cannot do the logically impossible, such as creating all possibles at once.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-ja'iz
Read the first Arabic sentence carefully. It states that it is permissible for God to bring into existence or leave any possible thing. Note the terms 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin'.
The term 'ja'iz' here is synonymous with 'mumkin' in this context.
2
Understand the paradox of creating all possibles
The passage explains that creating all possibles at once is impossible because possibles are infinite; doing so would lead to an absurdity (emptying the infinite). Observe the logical reasoning used.
3
Reflect on God's power and will
Consider the implication: God’s power is not diminished by not creating some possibles. He does what He wills, and what He does not will does not come to be. This is a key point of Sunni theology against those who claim God must do the best or create all possibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجائز في حق الله تعالى:
يجوز في حق الله تعالى إيجاد كل ممكن أو تركه، ففعل كل فرد من أفراد الممكن وتركه جائز، ولا يجوز فعل الممكنات جميعا دفعة واحدة، لأن الممكنات لا نهاية لها فكل ما قدر العقل جوازه فهو ممكن وفعله دفعة واحدة يؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له وهذا محال (٢).
ملاحظة دخل في الجائز بعثة الرسل فليست بواجبة كما قالت المعتزلة ولا مستحيلة كما قالت البراهمة والسَّمَنِيّة، ولا مكتسبة كما قالت الفلاسفة ولا ذاتية كما قالت الكرَّامية، فالبراهمة والفلاسفة ضلوا والمعتزلة والكرامية مبتدعة. ومذهب أهل السنة أن النبوة والرسالة فضل من الله تعالى.
(۳)
ودخل في الجائز ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي، ورؤية الله تعالى فيراه المؤمنون منزها عن الجهة والمقابلة والمكان وغير ذلك من الأمور العادية، فكما علموه منزها يرونه كذلك، فرؤيته تعالى جائزة وواقعة في الآخرة للمؤمنين وأما في الدنيا فجائزة عقلاً ممنوعة شرعاً الحديث لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا)، وأما
(۱) ما يجوز في حقه تعالى منحصر فيما ذكر . (ج) والممكن عند علماء هذا الفن كل ما حكم العقل باستواء وجوده وعدمه ويسمى جائزا ومعنى فعل الممكن إيجاد الله له ومعنى تركه إبقاؤه في العدم، وكل ممكن عقلا في حق الله تعالى فعله أو تركه ولا يجب عليه عقلا فعل شيء منه، ولا يستحيل عليه عقلا ترك شيء منه بل يفعل منه ما شاء ويترك ما شاء فما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن ، ويسمى كل فعل من أفعاله تعالى شأناً له قال تعالى ( كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ ) أي كل وقت هو أي الرب تعالى في شأن أي أمر يظهره على وفق ما قدره وأراده في الأزل من إيجاد وإعدام وإعزاز وإذلال وإغناء وإفقار وإعطاء سائل وإجابة داع وغير ذلك من شئونه جل وعز. (غ). (۲) وأيضاً إيجاد الممكنات دفعة واحدة يؤدي إلى فراغ قدرة الله تعالى أي عدم قدرته على إيجاد ممكن آخر، لأنه قد أوجده فتأمل (س).
(۳) قد يعرض لبعض الممكنات الوجوب لإخبار الشرع بوقوعه ويسمى واجباً عرضياً وواجباً لغيره وقد يعرض لبعضها الاستحالة لإخبار الشرع بعدم وقوعه ويسمى مستحيلا عرضيا ومستحيلا لغيره، فالأول كدخول الطائع الجنة، والثاني كدخول الكافر الجنة ، فإن كلا منهما من الممكن عقلا بالنظر لذاته، لكن صار الأول واجباً شرعاً والثاني مستحيلاً شرعاً لما ذكرنا (غ). (٤) رواه النسائي في سننه الكبرى عن عبادة بن الصامت عن رسول الله الله قال إني حدثتكم عن المسيح الدجال حتى خفت أن لا تعقلوه هو قصير فجج جعد أعور مطموس عين اليسرى ليس بناتئة ولا حجراء فإن التبس عليكم فاعلموا أن ربكم تبارك وتعالى ليس بأعور وإنكم لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا .
The possible in the right of God, exalted:
It is permissible (ja'iz) in the right of God, exalted, to bring into existence every possible (mumkin) thing or to leave it. So the act of each individual of the individuals of the possible and its leaving are permissible. And it is not permissible to bring into existence all possibles at once, because possibles have no end; so everything that the intellect deems permissible is possible, and their bringing into existence at once leads to the emptying of what has no end, and this is impossible (muhal). (2)
A note: The sending of messengers enters into the permissible; it is not obligatory as the Mu'tazila said, nor impossible as the Brahmins and Sumaniyya said, nor acquired as the philosophers said, nor essential as the Karramiyya said. So the Brahmins and philosophers are astray, and the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya are innovators. The madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna is that prophethood and messengerhood are a favor (fadl) from God, exalted.
(3)
And into the permissible enters the reward of the obedient and the punishment of the disobedient, and the vision of God, exalted. The believers will see Him, exalted, transcending direction, opposition, place, and other such customary matters. So just as they knew Him to be transcendent, they will see Him thus. His vision, exalted, is permissible (ja'iza) and occurs in the afterlife for believers. As for this world, it is permissible rationally but prevented by revelation (shar'an) – the hadith: 'You will not see your Lord until you die.'
(1) What is permissible in His right, exalted, is restricted to what is mentioned. (J) Al-mumkin according to the scholars of this art (ilm al-kalam) is everything whose existence and non-existence the intellect judges as equal, and it is called ja'iz. The meaning of 'act of the possible' is God's bringing it into existence, and the meaning of 'leaving it' is His keeping it in non-existence. Every possible intellectually in the right of God, exalted, is permissible for Him to do or leave. He is not obligated intellectually to do anything from it, nor is it impossible intellectually for Him to leave anything from it. Rather, He does what He wills and leaves what He wills. So what God wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be. And every act of His, exalted, is called a 'matter' (sha'n) of His. He said, exalted: 'Every day He is upon a matter' (Quran 55:29), meaning every time He, the Lord, exalted, is upon a matter, i.e., an affair that He manifests according to what He decreed and willed in eternity, of bringing into existence, annihilating, honoring, humiliating, enriching, impoverishing, giving to a petitioner, answering a caller, and other such matters of His, may He be glorified and exalted. (Gh). (2) Also, bringing into existence all possibles at once leads to the emptying of God's power, i.e., His inability to bring into existence another possible, because He has already brought it into existence. So reflect. (S).
(3) Some possibles may be subject to obligation due to revelation informing of their occurrence, and it is called 'accidental obligatory' (wajib 'aradi) and 'obligatory for another'. Some may be subject to impossibility due to revelation informing of their non-occurrence, and it is called 'accidental impossible' (muhal 'aradi) and 'impossible for another'. The first is like the entry of the obedient person into Paradise; the second is like the entry of the disbeliever into Paradise. Both are possible intellectually with respect to their essence, but the first became obligatory by revelation and the second became impossible by revelation, for what we mentioned. (Gh). (4) Narrated by al-Nasa'i in his Sunan al-Kubra from 'Ubada ibn al-Samit from the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, who said: 'I have told you about the Dajjal until I feared that you would not understand. He is short, with flat feet, curly hair, one-eyed, his left eye is obliterated, neither protruding nor sunken. If it becomes confusing to you, then know that your Lord, blessed and exalted, is not one-eyed, and you will not see your Lord until you die.'
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines and elaborates on the category of 'the permissible' (al-ja'iz) in relation to God. It begins by stating that it is permissible for God to either bring into existence or leave any possible thing. It then specifies that it is not permissible to bring all possibles into existence at once because possibles are infinite; doing so would lead to an infinite regress paradox—emptying something infinite, which is impossible. The passage then inserts a note that sending messengers falls under the permissible, refuting various erroneous views: the Mu'tazila who considered it obligatory, the Brahmins and Sumaniyya who considered it impossible, the philosophers who considered it acquired, and the Karramiyya who considered it essential. The correct Sunni position is that prophethood is a favor from God, not an obligation. Next, the passage lists other examples: reward of the obedient, punishment of the disobedient, and the vision of God in the afterlife. The vision is described as being free from direction, place, and other physical limitations; believers see God as they knew Him—transcendent. In this world, the vision is rationally possible but prohibited by revelation. A long footnote clarifies the definition of 'possible' (mumkin) as that which the intellect judges as equally possible to exist or not exist. It emphasizes that God is not obligated to do or leave any possible thing; He acts according to His will. The footnote also explains that some possibles become obligatory or impossible accidentally due to divine revelation, e.g., the entry of the believer into Paradise becomes obligatory, while the entry of the disbeliever becomes impossible, even though both are possible in themselves. The final footnote provides a hadith about not seeing God until death, supporting the prohibition of vision in this world.

Key Terms
جائز
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God must create all possible things to be all-powerful.
Correction: The passage explains that God may leave possibles uncreated; His power is not diminished. Creating all at once is logically impossible, not a lack of power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible for God to bring all possibles into existence at once?
A) Because God's power is limited.
B) Because possibles are infinite and bringing them all into existence at once would lead to an impossibility.
C) Because God prefers to create gradually.
D) Because some possibles are impossible by revelation.

Key Takeaway
God may create or leave any possible thing; He is not obligated to create any particular possible, and creating all possibles at once is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is under no obligation to create any possible thing, then He has no 'duty' to answer your expectations. Reflect honestly: are there areas in your life where you have felt entitled to God's intervention—health, wealth, or ease—as if He owed it to you? Will you repent of that entitlement and affirm His absolute freedom, even if He leaves your requests unfulfilled?
?
The lesson shows that logical impossibility (like creating all possibles at once) is not a weakness in God. But deep down, do you sometimes wish God's power were 'more' than what logic permits—like being able to do the contradictory? Search your heart: do you truly submit to the fact that God's ways are consistent and that His inability to do the absurd is a perfection, not a flaw? What assumption about God's power must you abandon today to worship Him as He is, not as you imagine?

Examples of the Possible: Prophethood, Vision, and Accidental Categories
Application of al-ja'iz to prophethood, vision, and the accidental categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of the possible applies to specific issues: prophethood, reward/punishment, and the vision of God, and how some possibles become obligatory or impossible by revelation.

Core Idea
Prophethood and the vision of God are possible, not obligatory; some possibles become required or forbidden by divine decree.

Having established the general definition of the possible, the author now applies it to specific theological topics. He shows that sending prophets, rewarding the obedient, and seeing God in the afterlife are all possible for God, contra various sects. He also introduces an important nuance: some possibles become accidentally obligatory or impossible due to God's promise or threat.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples of al-ja'iz
Read the passage listing prophethood, reward/punishment, and the vision of God as examples of what is possible for God. Notice the refutations of other sects.
The author refutes five groups: Mu'tazila, Brahmins, Sumaniyya, philosophers, and Karramiyya.
2
Understand the Sunni position on prophethood
The author states that prophethood is a favor (fadl) from God, not an obligation. This means God could have chosen not to send prophets; it was His pure generosity.
3
Learn about the vision of God
The vision of God in the afterlife is possible and occurs for believers. In this world, it is rationally possible but prohibited by revelation. Note the hadith cited.
The vision is described as transcendent, without direction or place.
4
Explore accidental wajib and muhal
Read the footnote explaining that some possibles become obligatory (e.g., paradise for believers) or impossible (e.g., paradise for disbelievers) due to God's decree.
This shows how revelation can change the rational status of an act.

يجوز في حق الله تعالى إيجاد كل ممكن أو تركه، ففعل كل فرد من أفراد الممكن وتركه جائز، ولا يجوز فعل الممكنات جميعا دفعة واحدة، لأن الممكنات لا نهاية لها فكل ما قدر العقل جوازه فهو ممكن وفعله دفعة واحدة يؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له وهذا محال (٢).
ملاحظة دخل في الجائز بعثة الرسل فليست بواجبة كما قالت المعتزلة ولا مستحيلة كما قالت البراهمة والسَّمَنِيّة، ولا مكتسبة كما قالت الفلاسفة ولا ذاتية كما قالت الكرَّامية، فالبراهمة والفلاسفة ضلوا والمعتزلة والكرامية مبتدعة. ومذهب أهل السنة أن النبوة والرسالة فضل من الله تعالى.
(۳)
ودخل في الجائز ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي، ورؤية الله تعالى فيراه المؤمنون منزها عن الجهة والمقابلة والمكان وغير ذلك من الأمور العادية، فكما علموه منزها يرونه كذلك، فرؤيته تعالى جائزة وواقعة في الآخرة للمؤمنين وأما في الدنيا فجائزة عقلاً ممنوعة شرعاً الحديث لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا)، وأما
(۱) ما يجوز في حقه تعالى منحصر فيما ذكر . (ج) والممكن عند علماء هذا الفن كل ما حكم العقل باستواء وجوده وعدمه ويسمى جائزا ومعنى فعل الممكن إيجاد الله له ومعنى تركه إبقاؤه في العدم، وكل ممكن عقلا في حق الله تعالى فعله أو تركه ولا يجب عليه عقلا فعل شيء منه، ولا يستحيل عليه عقلا ترك شيء منه بل يفعل منه ما شاء ويترك ما شاء فما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن ، ويسمى كل فعل من أفعاله تعالى شأناً له قال تعالى ( كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ ) أي كل وقت هو أي الرب تعالى في شأن أي أمر يظهره على وفق ما قدره وأراده في الأزل من إيجاد وإعدام وإعزاز وإذلال وإغناء وإفقار وإعطاء سائل وإجابة داع وغير ذلك من شئونه جل وعز. (غ). (۲) وأيضاً إيجاد الممكنات دفعة واحدة يؤدي إلى فراغ قدرة الله تعالى أي عدم قدرته على إيجاد ممكن آخر، لأنه قد أوجده فتأمل (س).
(۳) قد يعرض لبعض الممكنات الوجوب لإخبار الشرع بوقوعه ويسمى واجباً عرضياً وواجباً لغيره وقد يعرض لبعضها الاستحالة لإخبار الشرع بعدم وقوعه ويسمى مستحيلا عرضيا ومستحيلا لغيره، فالأول كدخول الطائع الجنة، والثاني كدخول الكافر الجنة ، فإن كلا منهما من الممكن عقلا بالنظر لذاته، لكن صار الأول واجباً شرعاً والثاني مستحيلاً شرعاً لما ذكرنا (غ). (٤) رواه النسائي في سننه الكبرى عن عبادة بن الصامت عن رسول الله الله قال إني حدثتكم عن المسيح الدجال حتى خفت أن لا تعقلوه هو قصير فجج جعد أعور مطموس عين اليسرى ليس بناتئة ولا حجراء فإن التبس عليكم فاعلموا أن ربكم تبارك وتعالى ليس بأعور وإنكم لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا .
The possible in the right of God, exalted:
It is permissible (ja'iz) in the right of God, exalted, to bring into existence every possible (mumkin) thing or to leave it. So the act of each individual of the individuals of the possible and its leaving are permissible. And it is not permissible to bring into existence all possibles at once, because possibles have no end; so everything that the intellect deems permissible is possible, and their bringing into existence at once leads to the emptying of what has no end, and this is impossible (muhal). (2)
A note: The sending of messengers enters into the permissible; it is not obligatory as the Mu'tazila said, nor impossible as the Brahmins and Sumaniyya said, nor acquired as the philosophers said, nor essential as the Karramiyya said. So the Brahmins and philosophers are astray, and the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya are innovators. The madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna is that prophethood and messengerhood are a favor (fadl) from God, exalted.
(3)
And into the permissible enters the reward of the obedient and the punishment of the disobedient, and the vision of God, exalted. The believers will see Him, exalted, transcending direction, opposition, place, and other such customary matters. So just as they knew Him to be transcendent, they will see Him thus. His vision, exalted, is permissible (ja'iza) and occurs in the afterlife for believers. As for this world, it is permissible rationally but prevented by revelation (shar'an) – the hadith: 'You will not see your Lord until you die.'
(1) What is permissible in His right, exalted, is restricted to what is mentioned. (J) Al-mumkin according to the scholars of this art (ilm al-kalam) is everything whose existence and non-existence the intellect judges as equal, and it is called ja'iz. The meaning of 'act of the possible' is God's bringing it into existence, and the meaning of 'leaving it' is His keeping it in non-existence. Every possible intellectually in the right of God, exalted, is permissible for Him to do or leave. He is not obligated intellectually to do anything from it, nor is it impossible intellectually for Him to leave anything from it. Rather, He does what He wills and leaves what He wills. So what God wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be. And every act of His, exalted, is called a 'matter' (sha'n) of His. He said, exalted: 'Every day He is upon a matter' (Quran 55:29), meaning every time He, the Lord, exalted, is upon a matter, i.e., an affair that He manifests according to what He decreed and willed in eternity, of bringing into existence, annihilating, honoring, humiliating, enriching, impoverishing, giving to a petitioner, answering a caller, and other such matters of His, may He be glorified and exalted. (Gh). (2) Also, bringing into existence all possibles at once leads to the emptying of God's power, i.e., His inability to bring into existence another possible, because He has already brought it into existence. So reflect. (S).
(3) Some possibles may be subject to obligation due to revelation informing of their occurrence, and it is called 'accidental obligatory' (wajib 'aradi) and 'obligatory for another'. Some may be subject to impossibility due to revelation informing of their non-occurrence, and it is called 'accidental impossible' (muhal 'aradi) and 'impossible for another'. The first is like the entry of the obedient person into Paradise; the second is like the entry of the disbeliever into Paradise. Both are possible intellectually with respect to their essence, but the first became obligatory by revelation and the second became impossible by revelation, for what we mentioned. (Gh). (4) Narrated by al-Nasa'i in his Sunan al-Kubra from 'Ubada ibn al-Samit from the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, who said: 'I have told you about the Dajjal until I feared that you would not understand. He is short, with flat feet, curly hair, one-eyed, his left eye is obliterated, neither protruding nor sunken. If it becomes confusing to you, then know that your Lord, blessed and exalted, is not one-eyed, and you will not see your Lord until you die.'
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines and elaborates on the category of 'the permissible' (al-ja'iz) in relation to God. It begins by stating that it is permissible for God to either bring into existence or leave any possible thing. It then specifies that it is not permissible to bring all possibles into existence at once because possibles are infinite; doing so would lead to an infinite regress paradox—emptying something infinite, which is impossible. The passage then inserts a note that sending messengers falls under the permissible, refuting various erroneous views: the Mu'tazila who considered it obligatory, the Brahmins and Sumaniyya who considered it impossible, the philosophers who considered it acquired, and the Karramiyya who considered it essential. The correct Sunni position is that prophethood is a favor from God, not an obligation. Next, the passage lists other examples: reward of the obedient, punishment of the disobedient, and the vision of God in the afterlife. The vision is described as being free from direction, place, and other physical limitations; believers see God as they knew Him—transcendent. In this world, the vision is rationally possible but prohibited by revelation. A long footnote clarifies the definition of 'possible' (mumkin) as that which the intellect judges as equally possible to exist or not exist. It emphasizes that God is not obligated to do or leave any possible thing; He acts according to His will. The footnote also explains that some possibles become obligatory or impossible accidentally due to divine revelation, e.g., the entry of the believer into Paradise becomes obligatory, while the entry of the disbeliever becomes impossible, even though both are possible in themselves. The final footnote provides a hadith about not seeing God until death, supporting the prohibition of vision in this world.

Key Terms
جائز
بعثة الرسل
رؤية الله
واجب عرضي
مستحيل عرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because God is just, He must send prophets and reward the righteous; this is a form of obligation.
Correction: The Sunni position is that prophethood and reward are favors, not obligations. God is not forced to do anything.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Sunni position on the sending of messengers?
A) It is obligatory for God.
B) It is impossible for God.
C) It is a favor from God.
D) It is essential to God.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood, reward, punishment, and the vision of God are all possible for God; some possibles become obligatory or impossible by divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that prophethood is a grace from God, not something He owes us? Does your heart accept that you are undeserving of guidance unless He wills it?
?
You affirm the vision of God in the afterlife. But what about this world: have you ever craved a glimpse of Him now, knowing it is forbidden? Are you content with His veil, or does your soul demand more?
?
Consider the concept of accidental obligation: what you are now required to do is only required because God decreed it. Does this humble you, or do you still think you deserve reward? How does knowing that even paradise is a gift, not a right, change how you worship?
