Definition of al-Ja'iz (the Possible) with respect to God
Said Foudah defines al-ja'iz and gives the proof that not all possible can be done at once.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what al-ja'iz means in relation to God and why it is impossible for God to bring all possible things into existence at once.

Core Idea
Al-ja'iz means God is free to do or not do any possible thing — except impossibilities like exhausting His power.

This lesson introduces the concept of al-ja'iz (the possible) in the context of divine attributes. It explains that God has absolute freedom to create or not create any possible thing, with the crucial exception that He cannot bring all possible things into existence at once, as that would entail an absurd infinite void. This passage from Said Foudah's commentary lays the foundation for understanding God's power and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the main points. Notice the definition and the proof.
2
Understand the definition of al-ja'iz
Identify that al-ja'iz here means God may create or leave each possible thing individually. It is not a necessity or impossibility.
3
Grasp the proof of impossibility
The passage argues that creating all possible things at once would lead to an infinite void, which is rationally impossible. Understand why that is absurd.
4
Reflect on God's freedom and power
Note that God is not obligated to create anything, and His actions are according to His will. The proof shows that His power is not exhausted by creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجائز في حق الله تعالى: يجوز في حق الله تعالى إيجاد كل ممكن أو تركه، ففعل كل فرد من أفراد الممكن وتركه جائز، ولا يجوز فعل الممكنات جميعا دفعة واحدة، لأن الممكنات لا نهاية لها فكل ما قدر العقل جوازه فهو ممكن وفعله دفعة واحدة يؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له وهذا محال. (۱) ما يجوز في حقه تعالى منحصر فيما ذكر. والممكن عند علماء هذا الفن كل ما حكم العقل باستواء وجوده وعدمه ويسمى جائزا ومعنى فعل الممكن إيجاد الله له ومعنى تركه إبقاؤه في العدم، وكل ممكن عقلا في حق الله تعالى فعله أو تركه ولا يجب عليه عقلا فعل شيء منه، ولا يستحيل عليه عقلا ترك شيء منه بل يفعل منه ما شاء ويترك ما شاء فما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن، ويسمى كل فعل من أفعاله تعالى شأناً له قال تعالى (كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ) أي كل وقت هو أي الرب تعالى في شأن أي أمر يظهره على وفق ما قدره وأراده في الأزل من إيجاد وإعدام وإعزاز وإذلال وإغناء وإفقار وإعطاء سائل وإجابة داع وغير ذلك من شئونه جل وعز. (۲) وأيضاً إيجاد الممكنات دفعة واحدة يؤدي إلى فراغ قدرة الله تعالى أي عدم قدرته على إيجاد ممكن آخر، لأنه قد أوجده فتأمل.
Al-ja'iz with respect to God, may He be Exalted: It is permissible for God, may He be Exalted, to bring into existence every possible thing or to leave it. So the act of every individual of the possible and its leaving are permissible. And it is not permissible to do all possible things at once, because possible things have no end; whatever the mind deems possible is possible, and doing them all at once leads to an infinite void, and this is impossible. (1) What is permissible in His right is confined to what was mentioned. And the possible according to the scholars of this art is everything of which the mind judges that its existence and non-existence are equal, and it is called permissible. And the meaning of 'doing the possible' is God bringing it into existence, and the meaning of 'leaving it' is keeping it in non-existence. Everything possible is rationally in God's right His doing it or leaving it, and He is not rationally obligated to do anything of it, nor is it rationally impossible for Him to leave anything of it; rather, He does what He wills and leaves what He wills. So whatever God wills happens, and whatever He does not will does not happen. And every act of His, may He be Exalted, is called a 'shan' (affair) for Him. He, may He be Exalted, said: 'Every day He is upon some matter' (Quran 55:29), meaning every time He, the Lord, is upon some matter, i.e., an affair that He manifests according to what He decreed and willed in eternity, of bringing into existence and annihilating, exalting and abasing, enriching and impoverishing, giving to a petitioner and answering a supplicant, and other such affairs of His, glorified and exalted. (2) Also, bringing possible things into existence all at once would lead to an exhaustion of God's power, i.e., His inability to bring another possible into existence, because He would have already brought it into existence, so reflect.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the concept of al-ja'iz (the possible) in relation to God. It begins by stating the general rule: God may bring any possible into existence or leave it. Then it specifies that each individual possible is equally permissible in terms of action or omission. It then provides a logical proof that it is not permissible for God to bring all possible things into existence at once, because possible things are infinite, and doing so would require an infinite void – which is absurd. Footnotes (1) and (2) expand on the definition of 'possible' and the proof. Footnote (1) defines al-mumkin as that whose existence and non-existence are rationally equivalent, and emphasizes God's complete freedom: He is not obligated to do or leave any possible, and every act of His is a manifestation of His eternal decree. It also cites the Quranic verse 'Every day He is upon some matter' to illustrate that God's actions are ongoing. Footnote (2) restates the proof: creating all possible at once would exhaust God's power, implying an inability to create more, which is impossible. The commentary follows the source order: first the principle, then the proof, then the clarifications.

Key Terms
الجائز (al-ja'iz)
الممكن (al-mumkin)
القدرة (al-qudra)
فراغ ما لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'ja'iz' implies that God has no will and is compelled to act or not act.
Correction: Al-ja'iz affirms that God has free choice: He may do or leave any possible, but He is not forced either way.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for God to bring all possible things into existence at once?
A) Because it would require an infinite void.
B) Because possible things are finite.
C) Because God's power is limited.
D) Because it would be against God's wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Al-ja'iz means that God is free to create or not create any possible thing, and He cannot bring all possible things into existence at once because that would imply an infinite void.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe God is free to do or not do any possible thing, but when your dua is not answered as you wished, does your heart truly accept that His choice was best? What specific thing are you holding onto that you need to submit to His will right now?
?
If every blessing from God is a pure gift because He is not obligated to give you anything, how does that change the way you thank Him today? Write down one blessing you normally take for granted and commit to thanking Him for it daily.
?
The lesson says God does what He wills and leaves what He wills, yet you often cling to your own plans as if they must happen. What is one future outcome you are anxious about that you need to release to His free choice? Make a concrete decision to let go of it in your prayer tonight.

Al-Salah wa al-Aslah: Divine Obligation or Possibility?
Sanusi defines al-salāh and al-aslah and refutes Mu'tazila obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that considering what is beneficial (al-salāh) and most beneficial (al-aslah) for creation is within the realm of divine possibility, not obligation, and how this differs from the Mu'tazila view.

Core Idea
Allah is not obligated to create the best possible world; that would limit His sovereignty.

The Mu'tazila argued that Allah must do what is most beneficial for His servants, making al-salāh and al-aslah obligatory upon Him. The Ash'aris respond that such actions fall under what is possible (jā'iz) for Allah, not what is obligatory. This passage from Sanusi's work defines these key terms and clarifies the disagreement, laying the groundwork for understanding divine justice and freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi first states the inclusion of these concepts under 'possible', then defines them, and then mentions the Mu'tazila opposition.
The footnote clarifies the Mu'tazila interpretation as individual-based.
2
Understand the key terms
Define al-salāh (that which is free from corruption) and al-aslah (that which is more beneficial than mere integrity). Ask yourself: why does the Ash'ari place these under 'possible'?
3
Contrast Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions
The Mu'tazila hold that Allah must provide the most beneficial outcome for each individual. The Ash'aris deny this obligation, asserting that Allah is free to act without any external compulsion.
This relates to the broader debate on divine justice (al-‘adl).
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider: if Allah were obligated to always choose the best, would that restrict His absolute power and freedom? How does this affect our understanding of trials and suffering?
This is a key difference that shapes much of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل في الجائز مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح ) خلافا للمعتزلة في قولهم بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح، والمراد بالصلاح ما ضده فساد والأصلح ما ضده صلاح. (۲) المقصود بالصلاح والأصلح عند المعتزلة أي بالنسبة إلى الفرد لا المجموع (س).
And included in what is possible is consideration of al-salāh (integrity) and al-aslah (most fitting), contrary to the Mu'tazila in their statement that al-salāh and al-aslah are obligatory. And what is meant by al-salāh is that its opposite is corruption, and al-aslah is that its opposite is integrity. (2) What is meant by al-salāh and al-aslah according to the Mu'tazila is with respect to the individual, not the collective.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines and clarifies the terms al-salāh and al-aslah, and presents the Ash'ari position that considering what is beneficial or most beneficial for creation is not obligatory upon Allah, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The author first states that consideration of al-salāh and al-aslah falls under the category of what is possible (jā'iz) for Allah, not obligatory. He then defines al-salāh as that which is free from corruption, and al-aslah as that which surpasses al-salāh in benefit. The footnote clarifies that the Mu'tazila understand these terms with respect to the individual rather than the collective, which is why they argue for obligation.

Key Terms
الصلاح
الأصلح
المعتزلة
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah must always create the best possible world for each individual.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that al-salāh and al-aslah are possible for Allah, not obligatory. Allah does what He wills, and His actions are wise and just, but He is not compelled to maximize benefit in every instance.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view presented in this passage, consideration of al-salāh and al-aslah is:
A) Obligatory upon Allah
B) Impossible for Allah
C) Included in what is possible (jā'iz) for Allah
D) Required for the collective only

Key Takeaway
Allah is not obliged to do what is most beneficial for His creatures; such considerations fall under divine possibility, not necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have faced a situation where you felt God should have given you a better outcome, how does this lesson's teaching that Allah is not obligated to give you the best challenge your sense of entitlement? What specific thought can you replace it with when that feeling arises?
?
Think of one person in your life who is experiencing hardship. According to this lesson, is it possible that what they are going through is not the 'most beneficial' for them as an individual? If so, how can you still trust that God's wisdom is perfect? What can you say to comfort them without implying God owes them something better?
?
This lesson distinguishes between what is possible for Allah and what is obligatory. In your own life, do you sometimes treat your expectations of God as if He owes you certain blessings? Write down one expectation you hold that you now realize may not be a right upon God, and commit to surrendering it to His will.

The Two Premises of the Kalam Proof
Sanusi presents the logical structure of the kalam proof with two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the classical kalam proof for God's existence, including the two premises and why one is considered theoretical.

Core Idea
The proof for God's existence rests on two premises: the world is originated, and every originated needs an originator.

This passage outlines the foundational logical structure of the classic kalam argument for God's existence. The author distinguishes between two premises: one that is nearly self-evident and another that requires careful demonstration. Understanding this structure is crucial for following the debates of theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from premises to conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage presents two premises: 'the world is originated' and 'every originated must have an originator'. Notice how the author describes each one.
The first premise is called 'theoretical' and the second 'nearly self-evident'.
3
Consider the implications
Why does the distinction between theoretical and self-evident matter? This determines where scholars focus their efforts to establish the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الدليل بالترتيب المنطقي هو كما يلي، العالم حادث، وكل حادث لا بد له من محدث، محدث ، فالعالم له محدث وهو الله الواجب الوجود، لأن المحدث للعالم يجب أن يكون غير العالم، والعالم ممكن فالله يجب أن يكون واجب الوجود، لأنه لو كان ممكناً لاحتاج إلى من يحدثه. واعلم أن المقدمة الأولى وهي قولنا العالم حادث نظرية ليست بديهية، أي تحتاج إلى إقامة البرهان عليها. والمقدمة الثانية وهي ضرورة وجود محدث للحادث، تقترب من البداهة ويمكن الادعاء بأنها بديهية، والاستدلال عليها يسير. فيبقى محل النظر هو المقدمة الأولى، وبها انشغل العلماء. وقد أقام عليها أهل السنة والمتكلمون أدلة قوية مذكورة في المطولات.(س).
Know that the proof in logical order is as follows: The world is originated, and every originated must have an originator, so the world has an originator, and that is Allah, the Necessary Existent, because the originator of the world must be other than the world, and the world is possible, so Allah must be the Necessary Existent, because if He were possible, He would need someone to originate Him. And know that the first premise, which is our saying 'the world is originated', is theoretical, not self-evident, i.e., it requires establishing proof upon it. The second premise, which is the necessity of an originator for the originated, approaches self-evidence and can be claimed to be self-evident, and reasoning upon it is easy. So the point of investigation remains the first premise, and with it the scholars were occupied. And the Ahl al-Sunna and the mutakallimūn have established strong proofs for it, mentioned in the lengthy works.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note on the logical structure of the standard kalam proof for God's existence. It lays out the two premises: (1) the world is originated (ḥādith), and (2) every originated thing must have an originator (muḥdith). The conclusion is that the world has an originator, who is Allah, the Necessary Existent. The author points out that the second premise is nearly self-evident (ḍarūrī), while the first premise is theoretical (naẓarī) and requires demonstration. The bulk of scholarly effort has been directed at proving that the world is originated. This lesson highlights the foundational logical framework.

واجب الوجود

نظرية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof is purely a priori and does not rely on empirical observation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the first premise ('the world is originated') is theoretical and requires demonstration, which involves empirical observation of the world's temporality and change.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which premise of the proof is theoretical and requires demonstration?
A) Every originated needs an originator.
B) The world is originated.
C) Allah is the Necessary Existent.
D) The world has an originator.

Key Takeaway
The kalam proof for God's existence depends on two premises, with the first (the world's origination) being the focus of theological demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The first premise (the world is originated) is theoretical and requires proof. Have you personally ever asked yourself why you believe the world is not eternal? Or have you just accepted it without reflection? What does this say about the firmness of your own foundation of faith?
?
Sanusi says the second premise (every originated needs an originator) is nearly self-evident. Is it truly self-evident in your own mind, or have doubts crept in from modern philosophy? What would it mean for your faith if you lost that conviction?
?
The entire proof rests on the first premise. If you were asked to prove the world is originated, could you? Spend a moment and try to articulate a simple proof. If you cannot, then your belief in God may be built on an unexamined assumption. How does that make you feel?

The Argument from Preponderance for God's Existence
The author presents the argument from preponderance for God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from preponderance (dalīl al-tarjīḥ) and how it establishes the necessity of a creator, Allah.

Core Idea
The argument from preponderance shows that existence requires a cause to tip the scales.

The argument from preponderance is one of the classical rational proofs for the existence of God. It examines why the world exists rather than not, and demonstrates that such existence cannot be arbitrary. In this passage, the author lays out the logical impossibility of something coming to be without a preponderator, and then elaborates on the six pairs of opposites that are essentially equal. Understanding this argument is foundational for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully along with the translation. Let the author's own words present the argument before you analyze it.
Notice the structure: a conclusion, a conditional argument, and a detailed note on six pairs.
2
Identify the main claim
The author begins by stating that the world is originated (ḥādith) and therefore must have a maker (ṣāniʿ). This is the foundation of the argument.
The maker is identified as Allah, based on prophetic teaching.
3
Understand the core logical step
If the world had no originator, then its existence would have preponderated over non-existence without a cause. But that would imply two equal alternatives are both equal and preponderant—a contradiction.
The key concept is 'preponderance without a preponderator' is impossible.
4
Examine the six pairs of opposites
The author lists six pairs (existence/non-existence, time, direction, quantity, attribute, place). Each pair is essentially equal, so preponderating one without a cause is impossible.
These pairs show the scope of the argument beyond mere existence.
5
See the conclusion
Therefore, the world must have a preponderator—a free agent who chose to bring it into existence. That agent is Allah alone.
This concludes the proof from preponderance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان العالم في هذه الحالة يكون حادثاً أي صنعة والصنعة لا بد لها من صانع وهو الذي ورد عن الأنبياء أن اسمه "الله" ، لأن العالم لو لم يكن له محدث بل حدث بنفسه أي ترجح وجوده على عدمه من غير مرجح، لزم أن يكون أحد الأمرين المتساويين في العقل مساوياً لصاحبه راجح عليه بلا سبب مرجح محال لأنه جمع بين النقيضين). ملاحظة : المراد بالأمرين الوجود والعدم، والزمان المخصوص مع مقابله من الأزمنة، والجهة المخصوصة مع مقابلها من الجهات والمقدار المخصوص مع مقابله من المقادير والصفة المخصوصة مع مقابلها من الصفات والمكان المخصوص مع مقابله من الأمكنة. فهذه ستة تقابلها ستة وكل واحد مع مقابله بالنسبة إلى الجرم مثلاً متساويان تساوياً ذاتياً، فترجيح أحدهما على مقابله بلا مرجح محال. لأنه جمع بين النقيضين فتعين أن للعالم مرجحاً أي فاعلاً وجوده على عدمه، وكذلك المتقابلات كلها ترجح الفاعل المختار ولا فاعل إلا الله.
And if the world in this state is originated, i.e., a product, and the product must have a maker, and that is what has come from the prophets that His name is 'Allah', because if the world had no originator but came into existence by itself, i.e., its existence preponderated over its non-existence without a preponderator, then it would entail that one of two things that are equal in reason is equal to its counterpart and preponderant over it without a preponderant cause, which is impossible because it is a combination of two contradictories. Note: By the two things are meant existence and non-existence, and the specific time with its opposite from times, and the specific direction with its opposite from directions, and the specific quantity with its opposite from quantities, and the specific attribute with its opposite from attributes, and the specific place with its opposite from places. So these are six pairs, each one with its counterpart with respect to, for example, a body, are equal in essential equality, so the preponderance of one over its counterpart without a preponderator is impossible, because it is a combination of two contradictories. Thus it is established that the world has a preponderator, i.e., an agent who gives existence to its non-existence, and likewise all the opposites preponderate the free agent, and there is no agent except Allah.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of God from the principle of preponderance (tarjīḥ). It begins by stating that the world is originated (ḥādith) and thus requires a maker (ṣāniʿ), who is Allah. The core argument is that if the world had no originator but came into being by itself, then existence would have preponderated over non-existence without any cause. This would imply that one of two equal alternatives (existence vs. non-existence) is both equal and preponderant at the same time, which is a contradiction. The author then explains the six pairs of opposites (e.g., existence/non-existence, specific time/its opposite, etc.) that are essentially equal, and thus preponderating one over the other without a preponderator is impossible. Therefore, a preponderator (murajjiḥ) must exist, who is the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār), and there is no agent but Allah.

Key Terms
ترجيح
مرجح
حادث
صانع
نقيض
أمرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument is about the world being temporally originated (ḥudūth) in a chronological sense.
Correction: The argument is not primarily about temporal origination but about the need for a preponderator to explain why existence occurred over non-existence, even if the world were eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the central contradiction that results if the world came to be without a cause?
A) The world would be eternal.
B) Two equal alternatives would be both equal and preponderant.
C) The world would have multiple creators.
D) The prophets would be wrong.

Key Takeaway
The argument from preponderance proves that a creator must exist to tip the balance from non-existence to existence, avoiding logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you learned this argument, did you have any doubts about God's existence that this proof addresses? Now that you see the logical necessity, what prevents you from having full certainty?
?
Consider the six pairs of opposites: existence, time, direction, quantity, attribute, place. Your own life is a specific combination of these. Can you sincerely claim that your existence at this moment is uncaused? If not, what does that demand of you toward the One who tipped the scales for you?
?
If this proof is sound, then disbelief is not just ignorance but a logical contradiction. How does that change your responsibility toward those who deny God? Write one concrete step you will take this week to invite someone to reflect on this argument.

The Proof from Mutual Opposites for a Preponderator
Sanusi proves the need for a preponderator (murajjih) from six opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator proves the existence of a Creator.

Core Idea
Opposites in creation demand a preponderator, and that is Allah.

Sanusi presents a concise yet powerful argument for the necessity of a creator. He points to the six pairs of opposites in the world and argues that if one opposite is favored over another without a reason, that would be impossible. This leads to the conclusion that there must be an external preponderator—a free agent—who gives existence to the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Note how Sanusi presents the six opposites and their equality.
2
Identify the core argument
The argument hinges on the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator. Understand why this is considered a combination of contradictories.
The key term is 'tarjih bila murajjih'.
3
Trace the conclusion
The final sentence states that all opposites necessitate a free agent. Note that this applies universally, not just to the world's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه ستة تقابلها ستة وكل واحد مع مقابله بالنسبة إلى الجرم مثلاً متساويان تساوياً ذاتياً، فترجيح أحدهما على مقابله بلا مرجح محال. لأنه جمع بين النقيضين فتعين أن للعالم مرجحاً أي فاعلاً وجوده على عدمه، وكذلك المتقابلات كلها ترجيح الفاعل المختار ولا فاعل إلا الله.
These six are opposed by six, and each one with its opposite with respect to the body, for example, are equal in essential equality. So giving preponderance to one of them over its opposite without a preponderator is impossible, because it would be combining two contradictories. Thus it is established that the world has a preponderator, i.e., an agent who gives its existence precedence over its non-existence. And likewise all opposites necessitate the preponderator who is the free agent, and there is no agent except God.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents a brief proof for the existence of a creator (murajjih) based on the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator. The author first posits six pairs of opposites (likely existence/non-existence, priority/post priority, etc.) and states that each opposite relative to a body is essentially equal. Therefore, if one opposite is chosen over the other without a preponderating factor, that would be a combination of two contradictories (i.e., giving preference without reason). Hence, the world must have a preponderator—an agent who chooses its existence over non-existence. All such opposites ultimately point to a free agent, who is God alone.

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjih)
ترجيح (tarjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument only applies to existence vs. non-existence of the world.
Correction: The text explicitly says 'likewise all opposites necessitate the preponderator', so it applies to all pairs of opposites in creation.

Checkpoint

A) Because opposites are essentially unequal.
B) Because it would combine two contradictories.
C) Because the opposites are infinite.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator proves that the world has a Creator, who is Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that for every pair of opposites in your life, God must preponderate one over the other. In what area of your life have you been acting as if outcomes are self-determined, rather than relying on God's choice? What would change if you truly believed that your next breath exists only because God preponderated it over non-existence?
?
The argument concludes that there is no agent except God. Does your daily schedule reflect that you recognize God as the sole agent of everything, from your heartbeat to your earnings? Where have you subtly ascribed agency to yourself or others? Will you repent of that today?
?
If preponderance without a preponderator is impossible, then every event has a divine cause. When you face a trial, do you see it as God's specific preponderance with wisdom, or do you feel it is merely random? What would it look like to surrender to His preponderance in that trial starting now?

Proof of Substances' Creation through Inseparability from Accidents
Sanusi proves that substances are created because they are inseparable from created accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that physical substances must be created because they are inseparable from created accidents.

Core Idea
Whatever is inseparable from the created must itself be created.

Sanusi presents a foundational proof for the temporal nature of the material world. He argues that every substance is constantly accompanied by changing accidents, and since those accidents are created, the substances that always accompany them must also be created. This argument relies on the principle of inseparability (mulazama).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the key terms: أجرام, أعراض, أكوان أربعة.
The passage is concise and each term matters.
2
Understand the four accidents
Identify the four accidents (motion, rest, conjunction, separation) and why substances cannot be free from them.
3
Grasp the central argument
Follow the logic: substances are inseparable from accidents -> accidents are created -> therefore substances are created. This is the mulazama (inseparability) argument.
The proof of accidents' creation is referenced but not given here.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Consider how this argument establishes the temporality of the physical world, a key premise in proving the existence of a Creator.
This connects to broader kalam cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فدليل حدوث الأجرام، أن كل جرم فهو ملازم للأعراض الحادثة المتجددة من حركة وسكون واجتماع وافتراق وهذه الأعراض الأربعة تسمى الأكوان الأربعة، والجرم لا يخلو من أحدها ، لأنه إما أن يكون متحركاً أو ساكناً أو مجتمعاً أو مفترقاً، ومن الأعراض الطعوم والروائح والألوان وغير ذلك، والأعراض حادثة ودليله تغيرها كما يأتي، والجرم الملازم لها حادث مثلها لملازمته لها.
The proof of the occurrence of substances (ajram) is that every substance is inseparable from the created, renewed accidents of motion, rest, conjunction, and separation—these four accidents are called the four universes (al-akwan al-arba'a). And the substance is free from none of them, because it is either moving or resting or conjoined or separated. And among the accidents are tastes, smells, colors, and other things. And accidents are created, and its proof is their change as will come. And the substance inseparable from them is created like them due to its inseparability from them.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument for the temporal creation of substances (ajram). It begins by stating that every substance is inseparable from certain accidents that are constantly renewed: motion, rest, conjunction (combination), and separation. These four are termed the 'four universes' (al-akwan al-arba'a). The substance is always characterized by one of these. Additionally, other accidents like tastes, smells, and colors exist. The author then asserts that accidents are created; their evidence is their change (to be explained later). Since the substance is inseparable from these created accidents, the substance must also be created—because whatever is inseparable from the created is itself created.

Key Terms
أكوان أربعة (al-akwan al-arba'a)
ملازمة (mulazama)
حدوث الأجرام (huduth al-ajram)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument relies on accidents being created in themselves, but the proof uses their change and renewal as evidence.
Correction: The proof explicitly states that accidents are created, and their evidence is their change. The inseparability argument then extends this createdness to substances.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must substances be created?
A) Because they are composed of atoms.
B) Because they are inseparable from created accidents.
C) Because they change over time.
D) Because they are observable.

Key Takeaway
The argument from inseparability (mulazama) shows that since substances always accompany created accidents, they must themselves be created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own body is inseparable from constantly changing accidents—your heartbeat, thoughts, and even your existence are contingent. If everything about you is created and dependent, what claim does the Creator have on every moment of your life? What is one thing you can do today to live in awareness of that dependence?
?
The argument shows that everything you experience is temporal and dependent on Allah. Are there any attachments in your life—possessions, relationships, or ambitions—that you treat as if they are permanent? How does this lesson challenge that, and what concrete step can you take to realign your priorities with the reality that you are a created being?

The Three Steps of the Proof for the Creation of Substances
The author lays out the three steps of the proof and answers potential counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical steps required to prove that substances are created, and how the author refutes each possible objection.

Core Idea
Proof of creation hinges on accidents, their inseparability, and the impossibility of an infinite regress.

The author presents the proof for the creation of substances (ajrām) as a chain of three essential claims. Each claim is met with a potential objection from an opponent, and the author provides a refutation based on observation or logical necessity. This passage walks through the dialectic step by step, showing how the proof stands firm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sub-proofs
Read the passage and note the three things the proof depends on: establishing accidents, their inseparability, and nullifying an infinite series. These are the backbone.
The passage begins with 'اعلم أن دليل حدوث الأجرام يتوقف على...'
2
Examine the first objection and refutation
Follow the first dialectical exchange: the opponent denies accidents exist beyond substances, and the author refutes by observation.
The refutation is based on universal human experience.
3
Examine the second objection and refutation
The opponent concedes accidents but denies inseparability. The author refutes by observing that substances are never free from accidents.
4
Examine the third objection and its refutation
The opponent argues accidents could be an infinite series, eternal in kind but temporal in particular. The author refutes by showing the kind depends on its instances.
This is the most subtle part; understand why 'hadith bi al-shakhs qadim bi al-naw'' is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن دليل حدوث الأجرام يتوقف على إثبات زائد عليها وهو الأعراض وعلى إثبات الملازمة بينهما ، وعلى إبطال حوادث لا أول لها، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يقول : لا نسلم أن هناك زائداً على الأجرام، فنبطله نحن بالمشاهدة، إذ ما من عاقل إلا ويحس أن لذاته شيئا زائداً عليها. فيقول : سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم الملازمة بينه وبين الأجرام، فنبطله بمشاهدة عدم الانفكاك. فيقول : سلمنا ذلك ، لكن لا نسلم دلالته على حدوث الأجرام ، لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة وذلك الزائد حوادث لا أول لها ، إذ ما من حركة إلا وقبلها حركة ،وهكذا، فتكون حادثة بالشخص قديمة بالنوع، بمعنى أن نوع الحركة قديم وشخصها حادث فنبطله بأمور عديدة ؛ منها أنه لا وجود للنوع إلا في ضمن شخصه، فإذا كان الشخص حادثاً لزم أن يكون النوع كذلك فبطل حوادث لا أول لها.
Know that the proof of the occurrence of substances depends on three things: establishing something additional to them, i.e., accidents; establishing the inseparability between them; and nullifying the possibility of accidents with no beginning. That is because the opponent might say: 'We do not concede that there is something additional to substances.' We refute it by observation, for there is no sane person who does not perceive in himself something additional to his substance. He might say: 'We concede that, but we do not concede the inseparability between it and substances.' We refute it by observing that they are never separate. He might say: 'We concede that, but we do not concede that it indicates the occurrence of substances, because it is possible that substances are eternal and that the additional thing (accidents) are accidents with no beginning, for there is no motion except that before it was a motion, and so on, so they are temporally particular (hadith bi al-shakhs) but eternal in kind (qadim bi al-naw'), meaning that the kind of motion is eternal while its particular instances are temporal.' We refute it by many things, among them: the kind has no existence except within its particular instances; so if the particular instances are temporal, then the kind must also be temporal. Thus accidents with no beginning are invalidated.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage provides a systematic breakdown of the proof for the occurrence of substances, showing it depends on three sub-proofs: (1) proving the existence of accidents in addition to substances, (2) proving the inseparability of substances from accidents, and (3) proving that accidents cannot be an infinite temporal series (hawadith la awwal laha). The author then presents a dialectical exchange with an opponent. First, the opponent may deny that there are accidents distinct from substances; this is refuted by direct observation (everyone perceives accidents in themselves). Second, the opponent may concede accidents but deny that substances are inseparable from them; this is refuted by observation that substances are never free from accidents. Third, the opponent may concede both but argue that substances could be eternal while accidents are an infinite series, each caused by a previous one, making accidents 'temporal in particular' but 'eternal in kind'. The refutation is that a kind (naw') has no existence apart from its particular instances (ashkhas); if all instances are temporal, the kind itself must also be temporal. Thus, the notion of an infinite regress of accidents is invalidated, and substances are proven to be created.

Key Terms
أعراض (a'rad)
حوادث لا أول لها (hawadith la awwal laha)
حادث بالشخص قديم بالنوع (hadith bi al-shakhs qadim bi al-naw')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that accidents could be eternal in kind (qadim bi al-naw') while being temporal in particular instances, allowing substances to be eternal despite being never free of accidents.
Correction: The kind (naw') has no independent existence; it depends entirely on its instances. If every instance is temporal, the kind must also be temporal. Hence accidents cannot be eternal in kind.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the opponent's claim that accidents are 'temporal in particular but eternal in kind'?
A) By arguing that the kind does not exist without its particular instances.
B) By observing that accidents cannot be infinite.
C) By citing scripture.
D) By saying that the opponent's claim is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The proof for the creation of substances requires establishing accidents, their inseparability from substances, and the impossibility of an infinite series of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a rational proof that everything you perceive—your own body, the chair you sit on—is temporally created. Do you live as if that is true, or do you act as if the world around you is permanent and self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized that none of it exists on its own?
?
The author says that every sane person perceives accidents in themselves. When was the last time you paused to notice that your thoughts, feelings, and bodily changes are not 'you' but accidental qualities attached to you? How does that shift your sense of identity?
?
The third step refutes the idea that something could be eternal in kind while temporal in its instances. Are there areas in your life where you treat a habit or pattern as 'just how things are' (qadim bi al-naw') while ignoring its actual beginning? What would it mean to confront the temporality of your own assumptions?

The Observational Proof of Accident Contingency
Sanusi presents an observational and analogical proof for accident contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of accidents is proven through direct observation and analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Accidents are contingent because we see them change.

Sanusi begins by presenting the most direct evidence for the contingency of accidents: we observe them coming into existence and perishing. He then extends this observation to all accidents by analogy, since they share the same nature as bodies. This lesson examines that foundational proof and a key remark about its validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening proof
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage: the proof is observation of change from non-existence to existence and vice versa.
Notice the direct empirical basis.
2
Understand the analogy
Read the analogy: unobserved accidents are measured against observed ones because they share the same nature (corporeity). What applies to one applies to the other.
This extends the proof to all accidents.
3
Reflect on the remark
Read the remark: the proof works if an accident persists for two moments; if not, change is by occurrence, not receptivity.
This clarifies a subtle condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل حدوث الأعراض مشاهدة تغيرها من عدم إلى وجود ومن وجود إلى عدم. وما لم نشاهد فيه التغير فهو قابل له ) ، ويقاس على الأول لتساويهما في أصل الجرمية، فما صدق على أحدهما صدق على الآخر. ملاحظة : هذا البرهان يتم إذا قلنا أن العرض يبقى زمانين ، وأما إذا قلنا إن العرض لا يبقى زمانين فكلها متغيرة بالحصول دون القبول. وتركيب البرهان أن تقول : لو كانت الأعراض قديمة ( هذه القضية ملزومةٌ)، لما تغيرت (وهذه لازم لها) لاستحالة تغير القديم وهذا بيان الملازمة، لكن نفي تغير الأعراض محال (وهي الاستثنائية) ، (بيانها) المشاهدة.
And the proof of the contingency of accidents is the observation of their change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. And what we do not observe change in, it is receptive to change, and it is measured by analogy to the first because they are equal in the principle of corporeity, so what applies to one of them applies to the other. Remark: This proof is valid if we say that the accident persists for two moments, but if we say that the accident does not persist for two moments, then all are changing by occurrence without receptivity. The construction of the proof is: If accidents were eternal (this premise is the entailer), they would not change (and this is the entailment) because change of the eternal is impossible, and this is the demonstration of the entailment. But the negation of change of accidents is impossible (and this is the exceptional premise), its demonstration is observation.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for the contingency of accidents. It begins by stating that the proof is the observation of accidents changing from non-existence to existence and vice versa. Anything we do not see changing is still capable of change, and it is analogized to what we do see changing because they share the same nature of being bodies. So what applies to one applies to the other. The author then includes a remark: this proof works if we hold that an accident lasts two moments; if not, then all accidents are changing by their very occurrence. Next, he gives the formal construction: the conditional premise: if accidents were eternal, they would not change (since the eternal cannot change). The entailment is that the negation of change is necessary. But we observe change, so the antecedent is false. This is a proof by contradiction.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
حدوث (contingency)
قياس (analogy)
بقاء (persistence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Not observing change in an accident means it cannot change at all.
Correction: The author states that anything we do not observe change in is still receptive to change, and is analogous to observed accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what is the main observation that demonstrates the contingency of accidents?
A) Their permanence
B) Their change from non-existence to existence and vice versa
C) Their similarity to bodies
D) Their lack of change

Key Takeaway
The contingency of accidents is proven by direct observation of change and by analogical extension to all accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You observe your own accidents—your thoughts, emotions, physical sensations—changing constantly. If these are contingent, what does that say about the permanence of your identity? Are you clinging to changing things as if they will last?
?
Sanusi argues that even unobserved accidents are contingent by analogy. What areas of your life do you assume are 'fixed' or unchanging but are actually subject to change? How does this challenge your sense of security?
?
If every accident is contingent and created by Allah each moment, then your present state—whether anger, joy, or health—is directly from Him. Does your heart feel that dependence? Or do you live as if your qualities are self-sufficient?

The Logical Structure of the Contingency Proof
Sanusi presents the formal logical argument for accident contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal logical structure of the proof for accident contingency, including the conditional and exceptive premises.

Core Idea
If accidents were eternal, they wouldn't change; but they do; so they are contingent.

After presenting the intuitive proof, Sanusi formalizes it as a conditional syllogism. He identifies the entailment and the exceptive premise, grounding the latter in observation. This lesson unpacks that logical construction, which is a standard method in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the formal proof
Read the Arabic passage starting with 'وتركيب البرهان' and its translation. Identify the conditional statement.
The conditional is: If accidents were eternal, they would not change.
2
Break down the entailment
The proof states the entailment (mulāzama): because the eternal cannot change, the conditional premise is true. Understand that this rests on the impossibility of change in the eternal.
This is the logical link between eternity and immutability.
3
Identify the exceptive premise
The exceptive premise (al-istithnā'iyya) denies the consequent: change is not negated (i.e., change exists). The proof of this is observation ('the observation').
This allows the conclusion that accidents are not eternal, i.e., they are contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل حدوث الأعراض مشاهدة تغيرها من عدم إلى وجود ومن وجود إلى عدم. وما لم نشاهد فيه التغير فهو قابل له ) ، ويقاس على الأول لتساويهما في أصل الجرمية، فما صدق على أحدهما صدق على الآخر. ملاحظة : هذا البرهان يتم إذا قلنا أن العرض يبقى زمانين ، وأما إذا قلنا إن العرض لا يبقى زمانين فكلها متغيرة بالحصول دون القبول. وتركيب البرهان أن تقول : لو كانت الأعراض قديمة ( هذه القضية ملزومةٌ)، لما تغيرت (وهذه لازم لها) لاستحالة تغير القديم وهذا بيان الملازمة، لكن نفي تغير الأعراض محال (وهي الاستثنائية) ، (بيانها) المشاهدة.
And the proof of the contingency of accidents is the observation of their change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. And what we do not observe change in, it is receptive to change, and it is measured by analogy to the first because they are equal in the principle of corporeity, so what applies to one of them applies to the other. Remark: This proof is valid if we say that the accident persists for two moments, but if we say that the accident does not persist for two moments, then all are changing by occurrence without receptivity. The construction of the proof is: If accidents were eternal (this premise is the entailer), they would not change (and this is the entailment) because change of the eternal is impossible, and this is the demonstration of the entailment. But the negation of change of accidents is impossible (and this is the exceptional premise), its demonstration is observation.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for the contingency of accidents. It begins by stating that the proof is the observation of accidents changing from non-existence to existence and vice versa. Anything we do not see changing is still capable of change, and it is analogized to what we do see changing because they share the same nature of being bodies. So what applies to one applies to the other. The author then includes a remark: this proof works if we hold that an accident lasts two moments; if not, then all accidents are changing by their very occurrence. Next, he gives the formal construction: the conditional premise: if accidents were eternal, they would not change (since the eternal cannot change). The entailment is that the negation of change is necessary. But we observe change, so the antecedent is false. This is a proof by contradiction.

Key Terms
ملازمة (entailment)
استثنائية (exceptive premise)
قديم (eternal)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof simply states that change is observed, without necessary reasoning.
Correction: The proof includes a conditional and an exceptive premise, forming a valid logical argument.

Checkpoint
What is the exceptive premise in the formal proof?
A) If accidents were eternal, they would not change
B) The negation of change of accidents is impossible
C) Observation is the proof
D) Accidents are contingent

Key Takeaway
The proof uses a conditional argument: if accidents were eternal they would not change, but they do change, so they are not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your thoughts, emotions, and physical states are constantly changing accidents. If they are contingent, what does that mean for your sense of self-sufficiency? Are you truly independent, or is every moment of your existence sustained by Allah?
?
This proof shows that anything that changes cannot be eternal. Your entire life is a series of changes. What are you clinging to that is inherently transient? How can you detach your heart from what will inevitably pass?
?
If every accident you experience—including your very consciousness—is a sign of contingency, then your existence is a constant proof of the Creator. How will you consciously acknowledge this reality today, in your actions and in your heart?

Refuting the Trilemma and Eternal Accidents
Sanusi refutes objections to the contingency proof via a trilemma and the principle of eternal non-annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that accidents may not really change from non-existence to existence by forcing the opponent into an impossible trilemma, and how he then blocks the further objection that accidents could be eternal.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be self-subsistent, transfer, or lie latent; nor can they be eternal.

Sanusi now tackles a key objection: what if what we call 'change' is actually an accident that was already existing in some form? He presents the opponent with three options — self-subsistence, transference, or latency — each of which is impossible. Even if the opponent concedes this, he might still claim accidents are eternal and merely change from non-existence to existence. Sanusi refutes this by the principle that the eternal cannot be annihilated.

Guided Path
1
Read the first refutation
Read the Arabic passage from 'دليل حدوث الأعراض' to 'فنبطله بأن القديم لا ينعدم' along with the translation. Notice how Sanusi sets up the opponent's denial and then forces the three options.
Pay attention to the logical structure: opponent's claim, Sanusi's trilemma, each option shown impossible.
2
Identify the four invalidations
The proof depends on invalidating four things. List them: accident's self-subsistence, transference, latency, and the annihilation of the eternal. See how the trilemma covers the first three.
The fourth is used in the second part.
3
Understand the trilemma
The opponent says movement after rest was not non-existent then existent, but existed before. Sanusi asks: was it self-subsistent, transferred, or latent? Each leads to absurdity. Work through each option's impossibility.
Self-subsistence of an accident is against its definition; transference would require it to be self-subsistent at the moment; latency implies conjunction of opposites.
4
Examine the second objection
The opponent concedes but says accidents might be eternal and change from non-existence to existence. Sanusi refutes by stating the eternal does not cease to exist. Why is that a valid refutation?
If something is eternal, it cannot become non-existent; but if it changes to non-existence, it would be annihilated, which is impossible for the eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل حدوث الأعراض يتوقف على إبطال قيام العرض بنفسه، وإبطال انتقاله لغيره، وإبطال كمونه وإبطال أن القديم ينعدم، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يمنع أنها تتغير من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه فالحركة بعد السكون مثلاً لم تكن معدومة ثم وجدت بل كانت موجودة قبل ذلك، فنقول له : هل كانت قائمة حينئذ بنفسها أو انتقلت من محلها لمحل آخر أو كمنت في محلها، فإن كان الأول لزم قيام العرض بنفسه وهو باطل، وإن كان الثاني فكذلك ، لأنه يلزم قيام العرض بنفسه في لحظة الانتقال، وإن كان الثالث لزم اجتماع الضدين وهو باطل. فيقول سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم أنه يدل على حدوثها لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة وتتغير من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه، فنبطله بأن القديم لا ينعدم. (٢) يمكن أن يقال : إن الأعراض التي شاهدناها في العالم منها ما شاهدنا حدوثه، كتحرك الأشجار وغيرها، ومنها ما شاهدنا أنه دائما متحرك ولم نره ساكناً، مثل القمر والكواكب، ومنها ما شاهدنا أنه دائماً ساكن ولم نر أنه يتحرك مثل الجبال. فالقسم الأول حادث بالمشاهدة، والقسمان الآخران كل منهما حادث بالقياس على الأول، لأن الجامع بينهما هو الجسمية، أي إن كل ذلك أجسام وأجرام، والأجرام لها نفس الأحكام، فما جاز على بعضها يجب جوازه على الآخر، فلما جازت الحركة والسكون على بعضها علمنا بالقياس القطعي أنها جائزة على ما لم نشاهد تغيره من القسمين الآخرين، وإذا ثبت جوازها على القسم الغائب عن حواسنا فيلزمه الحدوث. فافهم.
The proof of the contingency of accidents depends on invalidating the accident's self-subsistence, invalidating its transference to another, invalidating its latency, and invalidating that the eternal is annihilated. That is because the opponent might deny that they change from non-existence to existence and vice versa; for example, movement after rest was not non-existent then existent, but rather was existent before that. So we say to him: Was it then self-subsistent, or did it transfer from one locus to another, or did it lie latent in its locus? If the first, then the accident is self-subsistent, which is false; if the second, likewise, because it entails the accident being self-subsistent at the moment of transference; if the third, it entails the conjunction of opposites, which is false. He says: We concede that, but we do not concede that it indicates their contingency because of the possibility that they are eternal and change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. So we refute that by [saying] that the eternal is not annihilated. (2) It may be said: The accidents we have observed in the world include those whose contingency we have observed, like the movement of trees and others; and those we have observed to be constantly moving and have not seen them at rest, like the moon and planets; and those we have observed to be constantly at rest and have not seen them move, like mountains. The first category is contingent by observation, and the other two categories are each contingent by analogy to the first, because the common point between them is corporeity, i.e., all of them are bodies and substances, and substances have the same rulings. So whatever is possible for some of them must be possible for others. Since movement and rest are possible for some, we know by apodictic analogy that they are possible for what we have not observed changing of the other two categories. And if their possibility is established for the category absent from our senses, then contingency follows. Understand.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of potential objections to the proof of the contingency of accidents. It starts by stating that the proof requires invalidating four things: that an accident can subsist by itself, that it can transfer to another locus, that it can lie latent, and that the eternal can be annihilated. The opponent might claim that what we see as change is not really from non-existence to existence but rather the accident was previously existent in some form. The response is to force him into three options: either the accident was self-subsistent, or it transferred, or it was latent – all are impossible. Then the opponent concedes but raises a new objection: perhaps the accidents are eternal but simply change from nonexistence to existence? The refutation is that the eternal cannot be annihilated, so if it were eternal it could not cease to be. Then another objection: some accidents are always moving (like planets) or always at rest (like mountains) and never observed to change, so we cannot prove they are contingent by direct observation. The answer is that they are still contingent by analogy: all bodies are alike in being bodies, and what is possible for some (like change) is possible for others, even if not observed. The argument concludes that if change is possible for them, then they are contingent (since the eternal does not have possibility of change).

Key Terms
قيام العرض بنفسه
انتقال
كمون
عدم انعدام القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents might be eternal and merely change from non-existence to existence, thus not requiring a creator for their contingency.
Correction: The eternal cannot be annihilated, but accidents do cease to exist; therefore they cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the third impossible option Sanusi presents to the opponent?
A) The accident was self-subsistent
B) The accident transferred from one locus to another
C) The accident lay latent in its locus
D) The accident was eternal

Key Takeaway
The proof of accident contingency survives objections because accidents cannot be self-subsistent, transfer, or lie latent, nor can they be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own traits—like your temper or patience—do you treat them as permanent (eternal) or contingent? What would it mean for your spiritual growth if you recognized they can and must change?
?
The opponent in this passage refused to accept that accidents are truly new. Is there an area of your life where you resist admitting that a bad habit or a wrong belief came into existence at a specific moment, preferring to think it's always been there? How might that resistance prevent you from seeking change?
?
If the eternal cannot be annihilated, then what is truly eternal in your view? Have you ever attributed eternal, unchanging status to something that is actually contingent—like a relationship, a role, or an opinion? How can you realign your thinking with the fact that only God is eternal?

Analogy for Unobserved Accidents
Sanusi uses analogical reasoning to prove contingency of accidents never observed to change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves the contingency of accidents that are always observed in one state (like constant motion of planets or constant rest of mountains) by analogical reasoning from observed contingent accidents.

Core Idea
All bodies share the same nature; what is possible for some is possible for all.

The opponent might argue that some accidents, like the constant movement of planets or constant stillness of mountains, are never observed to change, so we cannot directly prove their contingency. Sanusi answers by a powerful analogical argument: all bodies are alike in being bodies (corporeity), so if change is possible for some accidents, it must be possible for all. Since change is possible for them, they are contingent (not eternal). This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on analogy
Read the Arabic passage from 'يمكن أن يقال' to 'فافهم' and its translation. Notice how Sanusi categorizes observed accidents into three types.
Identify the three categories: those seen to change, those always moving, those always at rest.
2
Understand the three categories
First category: accidents we directly observe to change (e.g., movement of trees). Second: those always observed moving (planets, moon). Third: those always observed at rest (mountains). The first is contingent by observation.
The second and third lack direct observation of change, so a different argument is needed.
3
Learn the analogical argument
Sanusi argues that all bodies share the attribute 'corporeity'. Thus, what is possible for some bodies (like movement and rest) must be possible for all bodies. Since movement and rest are possible for the first category (they change), they must be possible for the second and third categories as well.
This is an apodictic analogy (قياس قطعي) based on the universal of corporeity.
4
Apply the conclusion
If movement and rest are possible for the unobserved categories, then those accidents are contingent (capable of change), not eternal. Therefore, they too are created and require a Creator.
The possibility of change implies contingency, which entails temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل حدوث الأعراض يتوقف على إبطال قيام العرض بنفسه، وإبطال انتقاله لغيره، وإبطال كمونه وإبطال أن القديم ينعدم، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يمنع أنها تتغير من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه فالحركة بعد السكون مثلاً لم تكن معدومة ثم وجدت بل كانت موجودة قبل ذلك، فنقول له : هل كانت قائمة حينئذ بنفسها أو انتقلت من محلها لمحل آخر أو كمنت في محلها، فإن كان الأول لزم قيام العرض بنفسه وهو باطل، وإن كان الثاني فكذلك ، لأنه يلزم قيام العرض بنفسه في لحظة الانتقال، وإن كان الثالث لزم اجتماع الضدين وهو باطل. فيقول سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم أنه يدل على حدوثها لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة وتتغير من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه، فنبطله بأن القديم لا ينعدم. (٢) يمكن أن يقال : إن الأعراض التي شاهدناها في العالم منها ما شاهدنا حدوثه، كتحرك الأشجار وغيرها، ومنها ما شاهدنا أنه دائما متحرك ولم نره ساكناً، مثل القمر والكواكب، ومنها ما شاهدنا أنه دائماً ساكن ولم نر أنه يتحرك مثل الجبال. فالقسم الأول حادث بالمشاهدة، والقسمان الآخران كل منهما حادث بالقياس على الأول، لأن الجامع بينهما هو الجسمية، أي إن كل ذلك أجسام وأجرام، والأجرام لها نفس الأحكام، فما جاز على بعضها يجب جوازه على الآخر، فلما جازت الحركة والسكون على بعضها علمنا بالقياس القطعي أنها جائزة على ما لم نشاهد تغيره من القسمين الآخرين، وإذا ثبت جوازها على القسم الغائب عن حواسنا فيلزمه الحدوث. فافهم.
The proof of the contingency of accidents depends on invalidating the accident's self-subsistence, invalidating its transference to another, invalidating its latency, and invalidating that the eternal is annihilated. That is because the opponent might deny that they change from non-existence to existence and vice versa; for example, movement after rest was not non-existent then existent, but rather was existent before that. So we say to him: Was it then self-subsistent, or did it transfer from one locus to another, or did it lie latent in its locus? If the first, then the accident is self-subsistent, which is false; if the second, likewise, because it entails the accident being self-subsistent at the moment of transference; if the third, it entails the conjunction of opposites, which is false. He says: We concede that, but we do not concede that it indicates their contingency because of the possibility that they are eternal and change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. So we refute that by [saying] that the eternal is not annihilated. (2) It may be said: The accidents we have observed in the world include those whose contingency we have observed, like the movement of trees and others; and those we have observed to be constantly moving and have not seen them at rest, like the moon and planets; and those we have observed to be constantly at rest and have not seen them move, like mountains. The first category is contingent by observation, and the other two categories are each contingent by analogy to the first, because the common point between them is corporeity, i.e., all of them are bodies and substances, and substances have the same rulings. So whatever is possible for some of them must be possible for others. Since movement and rest are possible for some, we know by apodictic analogy that they are possible for what we have not observed changing of the other two categories. And if their possibility is established for the category absent from our senses, then contingency follows. Understand.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of potential objections to the proof of the contingency of accidents. It starts by stating that the proof requires invalidating four things: that an accident can subsist by itself, that it can transfer to another locus, that it can lie latent, and that the eternal can be annihilated. The opponent might claim that what we see as change is not really from non-existence to existence but rather the accident was previously existent in some form. The response is to force him into three options: either the accident was self-subsistent, or it transferred, or it was latent – all are impossible. Then the opponent concedes but raises a new objection: perhaps the accidents are eternal but simply change from nonexistence to existence? The refutation is that the eternal cannot be annihilated, so if it were eternal it could not cease to be. Then another objection: some accidents are always moving (like planets) or always at rest (like mountains) and never observed to change, so we cannot prove they are contingent by direct observation. The answer is that they are still contingent by analogy: all bodies are alike in being bodies, and what is possible for some (like change) is possible for others, even if not observed. The argument concludes that if change is possible for them, then they are contingent (since the eternal does not have possibility of change).

Key Terms
جسمية
قياس قطعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents that are always observed in the same state (e.g., constant motion of planets) are eternal because they never change.
Correction: Even if never observed to change, they share corporeity with changing accidents, so they have the possibility of change, meaning they are contingent, not eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the common point (الجامع) used in the analogy?
A) The accidents themselves
B) The fact that they are all observed
C) Corporeity (جسمية)
D) Movement

Key Takeaway
All accidents, even those never observed to change, are proven contingent by analogical reasoning based on the common nature of all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sky and see the moon or planets, do you ever assume they are self-sufficient and eternal? How does this proof force you to see them as dependent on the Creator every moment?
?
Consider the mountains you have seen—solid and unmoving. Do you treat them as permanent? What would it mean for your heart if you truly believed they could be changed by Allah in an instant? How should that affect your reliance on Him?
?
You are a body, like the trees and planets. What accident in your own life do you assume is permanent—your health, your wealth, your state of faith? How does this lesson expose that assumption as false, and what will you do to remind yourself of your contingency daily?

Principles 1-3: Accidents, Self-Subsistence, and Transference
Enumeration and proof of the first three principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three principles underpinning the proof of the world's contingency: that accidents exist as additions to bodies, cannot subsist by themselves, and cannot transfer.

Core Idea
Accidents are real attributes that cannot exist or move independently.

Sanusi begins by listing the seven principles for proving that the world is contingent and created. The first three principles concern accidents—their existence as additional to bodies, the impossibility of their self-subsistence, and the impossibility of their transference. These are foundational because they show that accidents are dependent entities, pointing to a necessary cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a sense of the author's listing style.
Notice the logical flow: enumeration followed by proof.
2
Identify the three principles
Find the first three principles in the text. Notice how each is defined and then briefly proved.
The proof for principles 2 and 3 is combined.
3
Reflect on the nature of accidents
Ask yourself: Why must accidents be additional to bodies? Why can't they stand alone or move? The answer lies in their essence—they are not substances.
This connects to the broader goal of proving contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الأصول التي يبنى عليها برهان حدوث العالم سبعة الأول : إثبات زائد على الأجرام فهو العرض الثاني : إبطال قيامه بنفسه الثالث : إبطال انتقاله الرابع : إبطال ظهوره وكمونه الخامس : استحالة عدم القديم السادس : إثبات كون الأجرام لا تنفك عن ذلك الزائد السابع : استحالة حوادث لا أول لها . ووجه الاستدلال على هذه الأصول السبعة باختصار أن تقول أما الأول : وهو إثبات زائد على الأجرام تتصف به كالحركة والسكون وغيرهما من الأعراض فهو ضروري لا يحتاج إلى دليل إذ ما من عاقل إلا وهو يحس أن في ذاته صفات زائدة عليها. وأما الثاني وهو إبطال قيام العرض بنفسه والثالث : وهو إبطال انتقاله فدليلهما أنه لو قام العرض بنفسه أو انتقل لزم قلب حقيقة العرض فإن الحركة مثلاً حقيقتها انتقال جوهر من حيز إلى حيز، ولو قامت بنفسها أو انتقلت هي لزم قلب هذه الحقيقة، أي تصير الحركة جوهرا وهذا باطل وأما الرابع : وهو إبطال الكمون والظهور فلأن القول بالكمون والظهور يؤدي إلى الجمع بين الضدين في المحل الواحد. وأما الخامس : وهو إثبات استحالة عدم القديم فوجهه أنه لو انعدم لكان وجوده جائزا والجائز لا يكون وجوده إلا محدثاً، فيكون هذه القديم محدثاً وهذا تناقض وأما السادس: وهو إثبات كون الأجرام لا تنفك عن ذلك الزائد فهو ضروري لأنه لا يعقل (۱) وهو الأمر المسمى بتسلسل الحوادث في القدم، وحقيقته تتابع الأشياء واحدا بعد واحد إلى ما لا نهاية له في الزمان الماضي، كما لو فرض أن زيداً أحدثه عمرو وأن عمراً أحدثه خالد، وأن خالداً أحدثه بكر وهكذا إلى ما لانهاية له، فقد تتابعت المحدثون واحدا بعد واحد إلى ما لا نهاية له في الماضي، وإنما كان التسلسل باطلاً ، لأنه يلزم عليه وجود حوادث لا أول لها ، وهو باطل، لأن كل حادث لوجوده أول، كما دلت على ذلك البراهين.
And know that the principles upon which the proof of the world's contingency is built are seven: First: Establishing something additional to bodies, which is the accident. Second: Invalidating its self-subsistence. Third: Invalidating its transference. Fourth: Invalidating its manifestation and latency. Fifth: The impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal. Sixth: Establishing that bodies are never free from that additional thing. Seventh: The impossibility of events without a beginning. And the way of inference for these seven principles in brief is to say: As for the first—establishing something additional to bodies with which they are characterized, like movement and rest and other accidents—it is necessary and does not need proof, for there is no rational person except that he feels in his essence attributes additional to it. As for the second—invalidating the self-subsistence of the accident—and the third—invalidating its transference—their proof is that if the accident were self-subsistent or transferred, it would entail the inversion of the essence of the accident. For example, the essence of movement is the transference of a substance from one space to another; if it were self-subsistent or transferred itself, it would entail the inversion of this essence, i.e., movement would become a substance, which is false. As for the fourth—invalidating latency and manifestation—because the claim of latency and manifestation leads to the conjunction of two opposites in one locus. As for the fifth—establishing the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal—its rationale is that if it were non-existent, its existence would be possible, and the possible only exists as originated, so this eternal would be originated, which is a contradiction. As for the sixth—establishing that bodies are never free from that additional thing—it is necessary because it is inconceivable that bodies be without accidents. As for the seventh—the impossibility of events without a beginning—it is the matter called the chain of events in the past, and its reality is the succession of things one after another with no end in past time, such as if Zaid is caused by Amr, and Amr by Khalid, and Khalid by Bakr, and so on without end. The chain is invalid because it necessitates events without a beginning, which is invalid, since every event has a beginning, as proofs have shown.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and brief explanation of the seven principles underlying the proof of the world's contingency. The author lists them: 1) establishing the existence of accidents as additional to bodies; 2) invalidating self-subsistence of accidents; 3) invalidating transference; 4) invalidating latency and manifestation; 5) impossibility of the annihilation of the eternal; 6) the fact that bodies are never free of accidents; 7) impossibility of an infinite regress of events. Then he explains each: The first is self-evident—every rational person perceives attributes in himself. The second and third are proven by arguing that if an accident were self-subsistent or transferred, its essential nature would be inverted (e.g., movement would become a substance). The fourth leads to the impossible conjunction of opposites. The fifth shows that if the eternal could cease, it would be possible and thus originated, a contradiction. The sixth is also self-evident because bodies cannot exist without accidents. The seventh is the impossibility of an infinite regress—every event has a beginning, so an infinite chain is impossible. This set of principles forms the foundation for proving that the world (bodies and accidents) is contingent and thus has a Creator.

Key Terms
أصل (principle)
عرض (accident)
جوهر (substance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the first principle (establishing accidents as additional to bodies) considered self-evident?
A) Because it is proven by scripture.
B) Because every rational person perceives that they have attributes beyond their body.
C) Because the Quran mentions it.
D) Because earlier philosophers agreed.

Key Takeaway
The first three principles show that accidents are real but dependent, always needing a body to exist in.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your anger, your joy—do you treat them as if they exist independently of your body and soul? How does knowing they are accidents that depend on a body change how you view your own states?
?
If an accident cannot transfer, then your knowledge of good and evil is not something that floats between you and another. It is yours alone. Are you truly accountable for what you know? How does this destroy the excuse of blame-shifting?
?
The passage says accidents are real but dependent. You are a substance: a self-subsistent soul. But your actions are accidents. Do you live as if your actions are mere temporary attachments that can be shrugged off, or do you accept that they define your moral reality?

Principles 4-6: Latency, Annihilation of the Eternal, and Body-Accident Inseparability
Continuation of enumeration with proofs for principles 4, 5, and 6.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand principles 4-6: the invalidity of latency/manifestation, the impossibility of the annihilation of the eternal, and the necessary linkage between bodies and accidents.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot cease, and bodies cannot exist without accidents.

Having established basic properties of accidents, Sanusi moves to three more principles. The fourth rejects the theory of latency and manifestation (kumun and zuhur), which would allow accidents to hide and reappear. The fifth proves that the eternal (i.e., God) cannot cease to exist. The sixth asserts that bodies are never free from accidents, a crucial step for proving the world's temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for principles 4-6
Focus on the concise proofs. Notice how each principle leads to an impossibility if denied.
The proof for principle 4 mentions 'conjunction of opposites'.
2
Understand the argument against latency/manifestation
Ask yourself: Why does latency-manifestation lead to two opposites in one locus? Think of a body that is sometimes black, sometimes white without a change in cause.
This principle is against the idea that accidents can disappear and reappear without a new cause.
3
Grasp the significance of principle 6
This principle states that bodies are always accompanied by accidents. This is crucial because if bodies could exist without accidents, they might be eternal; but they cannot.
This sets up the final step of the proof (the seventh principle).

?

?

Principle 7: Impossibility of Infinite Regress of Events
Explanation of the seventh principle: invalidating an infinite regress of events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh principle: the impossibility of an infinite chain of past events, which is key to proving the world has a beginning.

Core Idea
An infinite regress of events is impossible because every event has a beginning.

The seventh principle is the linchpin of the proof of the world's contingency. It shows that there cannot be an infinite series of temporally ordered events without a first. This principle, known as the impossibility of tasalsul (infinite regress), directly leads to the conclusion that the chain of events must have a starting point, which is the first created moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the seventh principle
Note how Sanusi defines the chain and why it is impossible. The example of Zaid, Amr, Khalid illustrates an endless sequence.
The key phrase: 'every event has a beginning'.
2
Understand the contradiction
If events had no first beginning, they would be infinite in the past. But each individual event has a beginning; therefore an infinite aggregate would imply a beginningless series, which is a contradiction.
This argument relies on the definition of 'event' as something that comes into existence.
3
Connect to the overall proof
This principle completes the demonstration that the world (all bodies and accidents) had a beginning and therefore a Creator.
The first six principles show that bodies are always accompanied by accidents, and accidents are beginning, so bodies are also beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an infinite past is possible as long as each event has a cause; they miss that the chain itself would have no beginning.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the chain's totality would be a collection of events without a beginning, contradicting the nature of events.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is an infinite chain of past events impossible?
A) Because it would require an infinite number of causes.
B) Because it would involve events without a beginning, but every event has a beginning.
C) Because the Quran prohibits it.
D) Because it leads to a logical paradox of self-causation.

Key Takeaway
The seventh principle shows that a beginningless chain of events is impossible, forcing the existence of a first event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event in your life has a beginning, what does that imply about your own origin? Will you today acknowledge that you are not self-caused and owe your existence to a First Cause?
?
Consider the chain of your daily habits: do you ever feel trapped in an endless cycle with no beginning? How does knowing that the chain of causes must have a start free you to seek the true First Cause of your actions?
?
You have just learned that an infinite regress of events is impossible. Are you living as if your life is just one more link in an endless chain? Or will you commit to tracing your existence back to its real Beginning and living in light of that today?
